Not by Birth, but by the Sword: A ÅšÅ«dra Big Man Makes His Mark

HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka was a singular hero (asahÄyaÅ›Å«ra) who, by the power of his might, made decapitated corpses dance upon the battlefield.1 One day (Åndu dÄ•vasam), this great warrior was reflecting to himself about the tales of other lone heroes of bygone days (purÄtanar) when he heard some subhÄá¹£itas being recited.2 It is a matter of some serendipity that one of these Sanskrit verses has survived in stone down into the present day:

Despite whatâ€™s been written down in a legal document [lit., even if it is written in the Å›Äsana], Fortune (Laká¹£mÄ«) does not go to the one who descends from a good family. Fortune (Laká¹£mÄ«) takes refuge in the sword; the earth is to be enjoyed by the vÄ«ras. Having abandoned what is not â€œsuitable,â€ he returns to the suitable thing. There is not any limit or law (maryÄdÄ) on the earth. The earth is to be enjoyed by the vÄ«ras.3

Hearing these words changed HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka’s life. As the text somewhat enigmatically tells us, when he heard the powerful people (ballavar) reciting these verses, â€œcommitment to the doctrineâ€ (abhiprÄyada naá¹ƒbugÄ•) of the powerful people (ballavar) entered his mind (manaá¹ƒbugaá¸·). For the first time, â€œthe merit of attainment arose in himâ€ (saá¹ƒpattugaá¸·ga puá¹‡yamÄ• mÅdalâ€™). For the first time, by the powerful people, who already discerned that their values were in accord with his own martial virtues (nijapratÄpÄnusÄrigaá¸·), he became recognized as one with the very same power. Succinctly, what our text is telling us,

[[P468]] in a surreptitious fashion, is that what HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka discovered in that moment is that his martial values and his virtues were the values and ideals of the ÅšÄkta Tantras. Guided by his own guru (Å›rÄ«mat) VÄmaÅ›akti,4 under the direction of this new source of inspiration, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka decided that a new temple should be dedicated to the lord VÄ«rabhairava, that it should be filled with beautiful new images, and that the consecrated focus of worship inside its inner sanctum should bear HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka’s own name.

Regardless of whether or not you are divinely inspired, temples cost money to build, they need resources to maintain, and their artful execution is dependent on access to a skilled labor pool. In this regard, at least at first glance, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka found himself in an opportune position. Among his other duties, his guru VÄmaÅ›akti seems to have been an important guiding authority among the artisan castes and was responsible for their ritual consecration as well as professional training. Indeed, much as it is today, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s hometown of Shirshangi was perhaps the most important of the ritual and cultural centers for artisan communities in the medieval Deccan. In fact, one of HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s fathers-in-law, a certain Teji Caá¹­á¹­agÄvuá¹‡á¸a, descended from the six original families into whose hands the Shirshangi land grant was entrusted so that the affairs of town would be justly administered on behalf of the KÄlamukha ascetics and their gods. In other words, our hero had all the right kinds of support from the local establishment to carry out his project. He just needed to gather up the resources.5

[[P471]] First, our inscription tells us, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka went and consulted with the artisans to discuss logistics and run a cost benefit analysis. â€œThe project is as follows; there is both brick and stonework. For each individual brick and stone [used in construction,] there is the granting of a thousand virtues and benefits. In either case, we find him taking the collection plate around to other notable people in the region. HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka purchases a plot of land from his father-in-law, Teji Caá¹­á¹­agÄvuá¹‡á¸a, who in turn then donates some of his own land to the temple complex to provide for an everlasting oil lamp in the new temple. The remainder of the contributions, offered both by residents of Shirshangi as well as visiting merchants and transient guests, take the form of the levying of nonvoluntary charitable contributions to the temple, mostly in the service of financing seasonal festivals,

[[P472]] such as the Caitra celebration of the god HabbÄ“Å›vara, as well as key moments in the Tantric ritual calendar, such as pavitrÄrohana, when initiates gather to renew their esoteric commitments and be absolved of their sins through the recitation of the samaya codes of their lineage before the image of the god. Surprisingly, the majority of these supplementary levies, obtained from local and foreign merchants, are extracted in the form of gold coins (hÄga). Indeed, one is left with the impression that, just as he had intended, the dedication of HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s new temple was a momentous occasion in the institutional life of Shirshangi, attended by a vast and diverse array of transregionalâ€”one might almost say internationalâ€” representatives hailing not only from virtually the entire spectrum of medieval Indiaâ€™s mercantile guilds, but also from a range of religious backgrounds.

I am profoundly grateful to my colleague and Kannada guru Tim Lorndale for the great dedication, erudition, and insight he has brought to bear in reading with me the long and difficult inscriptions in this chapter. I first became aware the significance for the study of Tantric knowledge systems of both of the key inscriptional texts used in the first portion of this chapter in 2015. I had set aside much of my time in the summer of 2017 for attempting to work on these sources with a range of scholars in Karnataka. In the end, perhaps because so much about these materials are irregular and uncertain, no one was willing to examine the work with me in a systematic manner. Feeling that these rich sources deserved better than merely picking out some pertinent details without considering the rhetorical structures in which those were embedded, I was delighted when Tim, who has among the finest senses of the grammar and the nuance of language of anyone in the academy today, committed himself to working [[P473]] with me on this project. All arguments about the text as well as any of its errors are almost certainly my own.

By the period under discussion, the AyyÄvÅá¸·Ä• 500, originally based out of Aihole, was one of the most important trading organizations in the world, dominating the commercial markets of Karnataka, greater Andhra, the Konkana, eastern Maharashtra, Tamil Nadu and parts of Kerala, with a sphere of activity that encompassed Java, Thailand, Burma and even mainland China, all places where inscriptions related to their activities miraculously survive. Our inscription also delineates the participation of a range of Jain mercantile authorities, perhaps not even from the Deccan, the transregional organization of nÄnÄdeÅ›is, active in Rajasthan as well as parts of the Indo-Gangetic Plain, as well as the ruling authorities of a range of autonomous mercantile towns (paá¹­á¹­aá¹‡a). Thus, quite apart from its significance as an expression of HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s personal religious vision, the installation of a Tantric esoteric deity provided an opportunity for the rather remote town of Shirshangi to serve as the preferred venue for an All-India trade convention in which merchants from across the subcontinent hocked their wares with the levies going to support the institutional life of the new temple. For Jains just as much as Åšaivas and ÅšÄkta-Åšaivas, participation in this event was inextricable from honoring the presiding authorities, especially the KÄlamukha preceptor VÄmaÅ›akti and the sponsor of the event, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka himself.

[[P474]] For the sake of the worship of the god HabbÄ“Å›varadÄ“va, there [in Hirisingi], they poured libation water (dhÄrÄpÅ«rbbaka mÄá¸i) to Ä€cÄrya VÄmaÅ›aktidÄ“va. SirikumÄranÄyaka, who is an honorable (mahÄvá¹›ddha) merchant and who is endowed with all honors, RavinÄyaka, KÄá¹­Å«ranÄyaka, Baá¸·á¸·ÄranÄyaka, Saá¹…ginÄyaka, RÄmadÄ“vanÄyaka, Paá¸apanÄyaka, Maá¹‡avÄla SÄ•á¹­á¹­i, Ä’á¸·inaá¹ƒbi SÄ•á¹­á¹­i, MalÄ•yaca SÄ•á¹­á¹­i, Koá¹‡á¸anaá¹ƒbi SÄ•á¹­á¹­i and the NÄnÄdÄ“si merchants, LÄá¸·a and Maá¸·Ä•yÄá¸·a, were all gathered in Hirisingi as a great assembly (mahÄnÄá¸u); the great [samÅ«ha] of the hero-merchants (bÄ«ravaá¹‡iga) granted one hÄga-measure for each horse load that is for the worship of that HabbÄ“Å›varadÄ“va that HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka installed.11

[[P475]] Thus, at the ritual center of this whole affair, we find a KÄlamukha guru engaged in activities connected with disciples hailing from two very different communities. On the one hand, almost unnoticed, are the representatives of the PaÃ±cavaá¹‡á¹‡igÄ•, who, as we shall see represent in the medieval Deccan â€œthe fifthâ€ caste of artisans. It is this group of people who design and built HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s temple, who produced all of the mÅ«rtis housed within it, and who also seem to execute the apotropaic function of chasing away potential obstacles at the ritual inauguration. In actuality, it is their efforts that laid the foundation for HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka attaining everlasting fame, and Shirshangi, as we shall see momentarily, was very much their city.

The inscription we have been exploring, which offers HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s testament, is inscribed on a seven-foot-tall stela in the center of the ritual complex at Shirshangi. It is placed at an almost equal distance between the two major focuses of worship. To its right lies the temple of HabbÄ“Å›vara, which preserves the images our warrior and his associates had commissioned and consecrated. The adjacent shrine, which predates HabbÄ“Å›vara, houses an impressive and exquisitely rendered life-size image of VÄ«rabhairava, which likely predates our heroâ€™s contributions. Today it also doubles as a storehouse for sacks of grain. In other words, much as it is today, the HabbÄ“Å›vara temple represents an incidental interpolation into a preexisting sacred landscape thoroughly dominated by a distinctly ÅšÄkta theology.

Shirshangi DevÄ«: the KuladevÄ« of modern ViÅ›vakarmans.

[[P476]] Seated in the easeful position (lalitÄsana) evocative of royal power, the goddess at Shirshangi represents one of the most spectacular examples of a visual idiom for representing what was certainly an esoteric ÅšÄkta deity inflected by a distinctively KÄpÄlika iconography.

VÄ«rabhairava: Consort of Shirshangi DevÄ«, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s kuladeva

Most evidently, the representations of ghouls in reverie encircling the base of her mÅ«rti as well as the image of the dog on the right rearing up and lapping up the blood that drips from the severed head in one of the deityâ€™s many hands not only replicate directly, almost certainly executed in the same hand, the details on her consort VÄ«rabhairavaâ€™s pedestal, in more general terms they invoke iconography that form a standard part of representation of Bhairava from

In light of evidence from Nepalese art historical sources, in which we find visual representations of the esoteric pantheons of the classical ÅšÄkta corpus that largely disappeared in the Indian context and that are clearly intended to serve as aides for esoteric visualization, certain features of the composition of the image are rather suggestive.12 Unlike the typical supple presentation of lalitÄsana, the goddessâ€™s foot is stiffly aligned with the very center of the base of her spine, almost as if she is engaged in mÅ«labandha. Her back is perfectly straight, and her eyes resemble the inward turned gaze of a yogin in Å›ÄmbhavÄ« mudrÄ. Somewhat more subtly, the modelling of the goddessâ€™s hands, which are more delicate on the left side and more dominating on the right, may well hint at the integration of Åšiva and Åšakti. The sword and triÅ›Å«la in her right hands and noose13 and á¸amaru in her left in their placement seemingly mimic the iá¸Ä and piá¹…galÄ nÄá¸Ä«s of the subtle body, and the negative space by the crown of her head is suggestive of the superimposition of the trident that is the hallmark of the daily ritual practice of the Trika Tantras. The name of her consort, VÄ«rabhairava, is associated in the surviving ÅšÄkta canon with a discrete body of theology of and ritual, nearly all of which is directly concerned with Kaula ritual worship that entails consort practices in the context of a melana with the yoginÄ«s. With the exception of a visualization of the deity possibly interpolated

[[P479]] into the unpublished Yogakhaá¹‡á¸a of the Manthanabhairava associated with the tradition of the Western Transmission, all the available sources, namely, the fourth á¹£aá¹­ka of the JayadrathayÄmala, the lost BhargaÅ›ikhÄ known to Abhinavagupta and Jayaratha, the ÅšrÄ«kularatnoddyota, the DevÄ«dvyardhaÅ›atikÄ, as well the eleventh-century paddhati of Vimalaprabodha, the KÄlikulakramÄrcana, are products of the pre-philosophical tradition that forms the ritual foundation of the KÄlÄ« Krama, a KÄpÄlika-affiliated form of Kaulism that incorporates into its theology and praxis, albeit in a subordinated manner, substantial elements from the system of the Trika.14 That such an association is not implausible is suggested by the explicit mention of related pantheons of deities, at sites where sadly the mÅ«rtis themselves have not survived, in virtually contemporary inscriptional record associated with other artisan centers in the Deccan.15 Not only were these spaces under the control of KÄlamukhas akin to VÄmaÅ›akti, but, as an extensive documentary record makes evident, the governing â€œsecularâ€ local authorities were frequently allied with the gÄvuá¹‡á¸as at Shirshangi. As we shall see, that the surviving literature on ÅšÄkta pratiá¹£á¹­hÄ in use in this region was openly associated with the JayadrathayÄmala offers tentative confirmation that this is the sort of scriptural resources that animated the ritual life at Shirshangi in the medieval period. Indeed, as we will explore in detail in chapter 6, these very materials were used by the KÄlamukha ÄcÄryas in training and initiating their artisans.

[[P480]] If this sort of text critically informed doxographical analysis is how the modern academic scholar of Tantra sets out to contextualize the types of evidence to which we now have access, in his inscription, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka himself proposes an almost entirely different sort of interpretive context for making sense of his world and the place that the veneration of esoteric deities plays in human history. Indeed, rather unusually, the non-documentary portion of the inscription reads like a work of constructive theology intent upon weaving a teleologically oriented narrative that explains how past circumstances converged to make HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka become the sort of person who could, â€œhaving performed that Bhairava Installation, which was the abode for Prasanna Bhairava. . . become famous.â€16 Towards this end, our text tells a rather bizarre story, explaining that HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s character and accomplishments are the inevitable consequences of descending from his family line as well as growing up in the special environment that Shirshangi provides.

HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka begins his story in the most unlikely way imaginable, by offering an explanation of how his family fell from caste and were expelled from polite society:

The incarnation of his line runs as follows (tadvaá¹ƒÅ›ÄvatÄravâ€™ Ä•ntâ€™ Ä•ndÅá¸Ä•):

In an excellent VaiÅ›ya family, when one man married a virgin girl from a ÅšÅ«dra family, they, too, became known as the â€œundesirablesâ€ (bayisu), since it is said that the world did not desire the son that was born to that householder. In that clan, there was a woman who was a padminÄ«. . . who knew [a special] way to produce offspring. One day, the one who arouses the three worlds, the Churning Bhairava (mathanabhairava), by means of his lÄ«lÄ, â€œplayedâ€ (ramiyisidaá¹ƒ) in that beautiful woman.17

Either because of a sexual encounter with the god himself or with a human sÄdhaka surrogate standing in for the deity, the padminÄ« rapidly becomes pregnant. Somewhat inconveniently for the narrative, the miraculous product of that union is a daughter named PadmÄvatÄ«. Thankfully, the divine virtues of superhuman male virility and female fecundity,

[[P481]] once infused into a blood line, are transmitted down through the generations. In turn, PadmÄvatÄ«, through divine virility (devatÄvÄ«rya) and unfailing virility (amoghavÄ«rya), bears a son named MallidÄ“va. Born by means of a boon (vÄraputra), he is said to be, in fact, the son of LÄ«á¸·Äbhairava. MallidÄ“va drives the â€œimpure thingsâ€ away from the Kiá¹£kindha mountains, establishing himself as the local power.18This best among the vÄ«ras dedicates all his family and extended kin network in perpetuity to LÄ«á¸·Äbhairava, who â€œat that time, became both the deity of their kula and the deity in their home.â€19 At this moment, MallidÄ“va was transformed into an ugramÄhÄ“Å›vara, a ferocious form of the special class of person whose status we have just explored in the preceding chapter. All of his subsequent descendants, we are told, are born with special auspicious marks,20 confirming their special collective destiny. HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s grandfather, a certain Hiriya HabbÄ•ya NÄyakan, seems to have blood line connections with this line.

Seven generations from the lineageâ€™s first recorded encounter with traditions of the BhairavasiddhÄnta, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s father, one of Hiriya HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s six sons, made his way to Shirshangi. There, despite being a noble representative of the fourth lineage (caturthavaá¹ƒÅ›adâ€™), in other words a ÅšÅ«dra, by subduing the enemies of the people, BÄcahuli became the local big man, taking for his wife a woman of the clan (kulÄá¹ƒganÄ•), also of

[[P482]] incomparable beauty and virtue.21 The text suggests that their child, our hero, was himself then conceived in a rather unusual fashion.

Having united itself, the whole world caused HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka, a treasury of heroism (Å›auryanidhi), and [about whom is said] that his power is overwhelming in its manifestation (atiprakaá¹­av), to come to life.22

Everything about this passage, as well as the one which precedesâ€”which uses many of the same terms of art we find in earlier parts of the genealogy which relate accounts of conception occurring in association with Tantric practiceâ€”suggests that HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka was not so much â€œbornâ€ as deliberately made. In other words, he is a product of a Kaula ritual rite designed to produce a superhuman child, an exemplum of the phenomenon that the great Kashmiri polymath Abhinavagupta, who also seems to have the product of such circumstances, labels being â€œborn from the yoginiâ€ (yoginÄ«bhÅ«). It is for this reason, as we shall soon see, that even before he awakens to a new kind of conscious self-understanding, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka functions as a living embodiment of many of the virtues associated with the Bhairava that is his god. If HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka was able to become a singular hero and ultimately Tantric virtuoso by virtue of his line of descent, the text he commissions is equally adamant in its emphasis on how the town of Shirshangi itself provided the perfect conditions for actualizing this potential. Again, the conditions that help fashion Shirshangiâ€™s inhabitants into exceptional people have their roots in the deep history of its environment. The name Shirshangi is a corruption of á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…gapura, a name it derives from being the birthplace of the sage á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga, a sacred

[[P483]] personage in the treta yuga who was also conceived under peculiar circumstances.23 Our text tells us that in ancient times, the sage VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka chose this place, in the shadow of the Kiá¹£kindha mountains, to perform his meditation and austerities. The sage was so singularly intent on achieving liberation, to the exclusion of all other objectives (kaivalyaphaá¸·aikÄá¹ƒká¹£Ä•yalladaá¹ƒnya phaá¸·ÄkÄá¹ƒká¹£Ä•yanuá¸·idu), that his intentionality came to suffuse the very landscape, such that animals like deer and serpents, which have a natural enmity towards each other, felt compelled to live in harmony.24 As the centuries went by, VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka produced within his body a vast storehouse of tejas, which took the form of amoghavÄ«rya. This profound vitality made him enormously attractive, so one day, when the apsaras MaÃ±jughoá¹£Ä was flying by, she became spell-bound by his body. Descending down to earth, the naughty celestial beauty reached her hand down into the sageâ€™s lap while he was deep in meditation and started touching what our text somewhat euphemistically calls â€œhis horn (Å›á¹›á¹…gÄ«).â€ When VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka became aware of this unwanted stimulation, the sage became enraged, came out of meditation, and cursed MaÃ±jughoá¹£Ä to turn into a doe.

As it so happens, it was the apsaras who ended up having the last laugh. Sometime later, still in the form of a female deer, MaÃ±jughoá¹£Ä dove into a pool of water and surprised the sage by surfacing nearby him. When he saw her captivating eyes, for some inexplicable reason, the sage lost all of his hard-won self-control. Ejaculating wildly, VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka quite literally saturated the landscape with a flood (vÄ«ryaugha) of many centuries worth of alchemically transmuted seminal fluid. In the process, much of his virility and merit became an integral and

[[P484]] lasting part of the Shirshangiâ€™s environment. In this way, it intrinsically became a place at once aligned with both esoteric spirituality and tremendous physical power.25

At the same time, MaÃ±jughoá¹£Ä rapidly became pregnant with this ill-fated coupleâ€™s unwanted child. When she gave birth to the boy, he emerged with an immense single horn (Å›á¹›á¹…gÄ«) growing out from the center of his forehead, evocative of his parentsâ€™ earlier initial encounter. Horrified, MaÃ±jughoá¹£Ä decides to expose her infant to the elements, hoping the sun would kill him.26 Though badly burned, á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga not only survives, but, apart from his other adventures, which our text passes over in silence, he grows up to become the surrogate force that makes possible the continuation of the solar race of Ká¹£atriya kings in the line of Raghu. Thus, when king DaÅ›aratha finds himself unable to conceive, it was á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga who provided the means that made possible the birth of RÄma, Laká¹£maá¹‡a, Åšatrughna, and Bharata. Indeed, as the inscription tells us, enroute back from slaying the demon king RÄvaá¹‡a, RÄma and Laká¹£maá¹‡a, accompanied by Hanuman, made a point of stopping in the village, and in the spirit of the ÅšivadharmaÅ›Ästra, set out consecrating liá¹…gas in the domain that made their conception possible in the first place.27

[[P485]] Though we have only begun to scratch the surface of the rich literary character and fascinating philological texture of the central inscription at Shirshangi, for our purposes, before we return to thinking about how HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka fits into the picture we have been assembling, it is best to briefly attend to the larger conceptual frameworks that the text presents, many of which will prove integral to understanding the world of the medieval Deccan across a wide range of media, linguistic registers, and rungs within the social hierarchy. Two points are particularly salient. The first is that the circumstances in which a thing or person comes into being largely shape its character and incline it to a certain kind of nature. Nowhere is such a dynamic more perfectly instantiated than in the case of á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga, whose defining feature, the Å›á¹›á¹…ga on his forehead, is the direct by product of his motherâ€™s violating touch of his father VibhÄá¹‡á¸akaâ€™s rather different sort of â€œhornâ€ in the instant before his otherwise inconceivable conception. Thus, while it is easy to see how such first principles could prove synergistic with the logic of caste, imagined as type of biological-spiritual determinism that distributes value and purpose along predetermined lines, as the examples we have been exploring make evident, this logic is neither reducible to caste frameworks nor is it necessarily an ideological byproduct of such thinking that inevitably reinforces given strictures.

[[P486]] Indeed, in the same way that the circumstances of conception structure to some degree a human destiny, the same principle holds true when it comes the production of objects of material culture, the construction of buildings, and even the land itself. Much as we can think of vyavasthÄ, the differential application of site-specific rules in the legal realm, as pertaining to specific human communities in a manner that assigns to them sometimes mutually incommensurable ways of being in the world aligned with distinct principles, our sources imagine a sort of vyavasthÄ, operating upon the land itself that makes specific sites fit for specific behaviors. Significantly, as our Pratiá¹£á¹­hÄ Tantras will explore in greater detail, not only does the presence of saints and supernatural beings for a sustained period of time in a single place create â€œsacred space,â€ but what it generates, based on the internal condition and mental state of the agent in question, are radically different types of energetically charged spaces aligned to the cultivation of different human and nonhuman propensities and emotions. On a more mundane level, the same logic structures the activities of artisan castes, especially when they are involved in designing temples or other forms of urban planning. Much in the same manner as is somewhat more familiar from the popular reception of Chinese traditions of Feng Shuâ€™i, different quadrants of the space inside and surrounding the future building are conceived of being more or else suited to distinct types of activities and of bearing varying degrees of auspiciousness and inauspiciousness, with all of these features being further inflected by calendrical concerns and changing temporal conditions. An integral dimension of designing a building or town thus entails choosing a space that has a minimum number of inauspicious spaces and situating the building in a manner so as to maximize the alignment of its intended function with the innate character of the surrounding space. In essence, in the medieval Deccan there is no such thing as neutral or common space; for all spaces are conceptualized as internally differentiated to some degree in their nature and function.

[[P487]] The second principle that our text makes evident is that even in hyper-specialized domains, like the sacred field of Shirshangi, multiple systems of meaning are constantly at play addressing a range of intended audiences. Under such circumstances, a single story, image, or event is necessarily inflected in a manner that intentionally communicates a range of discrete meanings. In light of the unmistakably Tantric conceptual systematicities evident in other portions of our inscription, the manner in which the retelling of the stories of VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka and á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga seemingly invoke such resonances is most likely not accidental. Instead of generically engaging in austerities, as in PurÄá¹‡ic and Epic sources, and thereby threatening the god Indra in his quest for power, VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka is depicted as a soteriologically oriented Tantric yogin, generating the very same power of unfailing virility (amoghavÄ«rya) that the rest of the inscription associates with both Bhairava and his devotee HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka.

The descent of the apsaras MaÃ±jughoá¹£Äâ€”beautiful soundâ€”taking the place of the epic UrvaÅ›Ä«, who no longer serves as messenger from the gods but is propelled by her own will and desire, is framed to resemble the arrival of a yoginÄ« attracted to the life force of the adept.28 When VibhÄá¹‡á¸aka succumbs to her charms, in a manner that mirrors the rhetoric of ÅšÄkta revelation, he releases a veritable flood (ogha/augha) of his power and potential, which in perpetuity is transmitted to those who are fit to receive it. Confirming such connections, in Shirshangi today the space where â€œthe á¹›á¹£i did his practiceâ€ is located a mile or so down the road from the main temple complex. In the shadow of the Kiá¹£kindha mountains, one passes an image of KÄlÄ« and a large well full of brackish water and then finds oneself entering a series of caves now called Siddheshwar Gumphe. Entering the main cavern, the explorer is confronted with images of seven mothers, a Gaá¹‡eÅ›a, a Viá¹£á¹‡u, as well as a large liá¹…ga, all done in the same exceptionally skilled hand that executed the images in the HabbÄ“Å›vara temple.

[[P491]] As we can see, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s inscription at the center of Shirshangi begins with an act of reverence that at the same time functions as constructive theology. On the surface, we are told that HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka owes everything he has and everything he is to his god. And yet, the very act of delineating what makes Bhairava great provides the reader with a point of reference for fostering the growing recognition, mirroring that of our hero, that HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka himself embodies these self-same virtues. After the praise of Bhairava, the text offers a celebration of the KuntaladÄ“Å›a, a lock of hair on the young beauty that is India, and then introduces the peerless place called á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga by telling us the story of its origins. It is only after thirty verses recounting the stories we have already explored that the inscription reintroduces our hero. Before we learn anything else about him, we are informed that the everlasting land of á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga is a field for the heroes (vÄ«raká¹£Ä“tra) where the four codes of samaya are observed and upheld (catussamÄ•ya samuddharaá¹‡ar) so long as the sun and the moon will persist.30 The nÄyaka who carries out this task of protecting the place (abhiraká¹£isu) is a treasure house of heroic virtue and fortitude (Å›auryadhairyanidhi) and he has the name HabbÄ•ya. In essence, virtually everything we learn about this human warlord is designed to foster the recognition in the audience that the god and his devotee do the same sorts of things and have the same nature.

[[P492]] Indeed, just as the god Prasannabhairava is entreated to give his devotee whatever he desires (kuá¸ugÄ• Ä«psitÄrtthamaá¹ƒ) to the human beings who depend upon him HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka also bestows what is desired (abhÄ«á¹£á¹­amaá¹ƒ kuá¸uva).31 HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s journey of self-discovery, which culminates in the moment when he hears the subhÄá¹£ita proclaiming, â€œthe vÄ«ra must enjoy the earth,â€ amounts to recognizing that being a warrior and being a vÄ«ra are two related but different things. In the somewhat obscure language of the medieval Deccan, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka initially saw himself as simply a distillation of alpha (gaá¹‡á¸a) male virtues.

If one were to say (Ä•nalu): â€œ who (Är) are the best men (aggada gaá¹‡á¸ar) among the heroes of the world-famous Kuntala?â€ They are the sitagara gaá¹‡á¸a. If one were to say: â€œwho are the men who subdue the power of thieves?â€ They are the sitagara gaá¹‡á¸a; If one were to say: â€œwho are the men who fight the opposing enemies?â€ They are the sitagara gaá¹‡á¸a. Heroism, of this type (sitagara gaá¹‡á¸a), will become formidable in HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka.32

The alpha male (gaá¹‡á¸a) who chases away the despicable people who prey upon the towns folk and the steal the livestock is both a social identity with its own ethos as well as a designated professional function of providing protection (pÄlana) originally associated with the Ká¹£atriya class in the DharmaÅ›Ästra literature. In this role, wielding a bow, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka repeatedly rode into battle, acting not on behalf of some foreign lord, but in the service of maintaining the autonomy of his community, which, as an aká¹£ayavá¹›tti nominally governed by religious authorities, does not fall under the purview of the state. Indeed, he boasts, somewhat mysteriously, that he eschews the arrogance that comes with royal titles,33 instead himself

[[P493]] offering protection to those who seek refuge. Our inscription offers us fascinating glimpses of this stern and magnanimous paternalistic ideology. On the one hand, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka never lies, nor does he suffer those around him to utter anything other than the truth.34 He is merciless in battle to his enemies, and yet at the same time, if those he has defeated attempt to surrender, apparently unlike other warriors who ridicule and then butcher prisoners of war, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka lets them take sanctuary. Turning his attention towards the warriors in his own band who disparaged these codes of honor, he skewers such honorless men on the spot with his sword.35 But heroism of â€œthis typeâ€ is something different than the true patrimony offered him by his lineage. Like all those who observe Åšivaâ€™s dharma as it is taught in the ÅšivadharmaÅ›Ästra, from the moment we meet him, HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka is introduced to us as an incarnation of Rudra (rudrÄvatÄra), in this case, specifically instantiating the pramathagaá¹‡a NÄ«lalohita.36But like his grandsire MallidÄ“va before him, HabbÄ•ya is both a ferocious and great lord (mahÄ-Ä«Å›vara), in other words a maheÅ›vara in the Åšivadharmaâ€™s own terms, as well as an ugramaheÅ›vara, a terrifying god on earth who bears the blood of Bhairava in his veins, whose storehouse of heroic virtue (Å›auryanidhi) was with him from the moment of conception.

Once HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka discovers that the lord he has worshipped as HabbÄ“Å›vara is not merely the family deity of his clan, but is in fact the same esoteric Bhairava whose power and grace underwrites both the ritual center at the heart of his home and the esoteric theology of ÄcÄryas who preside over the land he protects, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s sense of what his dharma entails becomes more expansive. Like his eternal god, HabbÄ•ya wants to ensure that he is able

[[P494]] to carry out his duties as protector in perpetuity. And so, out of the goodness of his heart or mind (sumanasadoá¸·), HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka himself provides the vá¹›tti for a temple at once dedicated to the god of his clan and the lord of the universe who rescued his line from untouchability.37 This is to say, somewhat in the matter of an endowed university chair, he arranges the funding and logistics for multigenerational economic support of the range of professionals, ritualists, dancers, teachers, attendants, that make a temple function. In this way, even after he dies, through the medium of the god he installs he can continue, in a palpable, concrete way, to protect and preserve the people of the region where his family found refuge.

When HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka â€œmakes the virtuous offering (puá¹ƒá¹‡yada baá¸·iv),â€ quite often a euphemism for a human sacrifice, he â€œobtains in lasting form all the virtuesâ€ (puá¹ƒá¹‡yaá¹ƒgaá¸·uá¹ƒ dÅrÄ•kÅá¸·).38 At this moment of supposed spiritual realization, it becomes possible for his name to become the name of the god, for the two have become one.39 And so he declares before the vast assembled public, â€œÄªÅ›vara alone will become manifestâ€ (Ä«Å›varanÄ•y akkuv Ä•mbudan), and performs the installation of the god. In short, what our text seems to offer is a precious example of the popular reception of the esoteric teachings of the ÅšÄkta and especially Kaula Tantras. Much as we saw in the preceding chapter, it offers us palpable proof that teachings of these texts that called into question the ontological foundation of the social order as rooted in questions of caste had a real-world impact. It is difficult to imagine a BrÄhmaá¹‡ical ÄgrahÄra playing host to a vast international trade convention comprised primarily of wealthy ÅšÅ«dras and VaiÅ›yas from a range of backgrounds and religious identities. It is unfathomable that such an affair would be presided over by antinomian yogins under the direction of a ÅšÅ«dra warrior

[[P495]] who proudly introduces himself by telling the story of how his family was outcaste in a prior generation, an event that in the end proved serendipitous, for it made possible their esoteric initiations and his own liberation. At the same time, HabbÄ•ya NÄyakaâ€™s testament offers a fascinating quotidian reception relating to the teaching in late Kaula sources of the essential unity of self with the supreme reality in accordance with that classic dictum â€œin order to worship the god, one must be become the god.â€

Whatever He Says Becomes Mantra: The KÄlamukhas and the ÅšÄkta Tantras

On the very bottom of the large stela, the artisan SÄtoja tells us, the Å›Äsana at Shirshangi has been written in modern Kannada (pÅsagaá¹ƒnaá¸a). As we have witnessed, under the direction of the singular HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka, the poet Sajanatilaka, whose work we have been reading, crafted a text of striking originality that repeatedly bends preexisting conventions in service to new ends. In a rather surprising manner, however, for his second maá¹…gala verse to Prasannabhairava, instead of composing something â€œnew,â€ Sajanatilaka chose to simply recycle someone elseâ€™s composition, a pattern that continues on and off for the next five verses.

The beam from his third eye is like the submarine fire. His mass of dreadlocks is like coral. He is adorned with a snake like the sun that has set.42 His necklace of skulls is

Just like the form of the auspicious play of the blissful Bhairava/the Bhairava who belongs to the Goddess KÄá¸·ikÄ.

Incongruously, given that in both cases we are speaking of landlocked sites a thousand miles from the seashore, both of these verses systematically homologize the traits of ferocious Bhairava with the profound power of the sea. Indeed, despite the fact that they are dedicated to different forms of Bhairava, the inscription at Shirshangi offers a virtually verbatim reproduction of its predecessor. Given that in neither case does the verse in question pertain in any recognizable fashion to the specific form of the deity under worship at these places, it seems plausible to suggest that neither is the original. Instead, what we are dealing with are traces of otherwise lost chancellery conventions for the representing of ÅšÄkta deities. In other words, places like Shirshangi were not quaint outliers operating in relative isolation. They were important nodes of circulation integral to a wider discursive pattern in which heavily networked esoteric centers effectively defined the medieval Deccan.

Despite the fact that HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka managed to host a veritable â€œWhoâ€™s Whoâ€ of the international trade establishment of his day, the extensive documentary records associated with Kukkunur and the originary settlers of this sacred center offers a much more promising archive for beginning the process of reconstructing these important dynamics. Thus, in an inscription from 956 CE, at the ritual center of Bagali, fifty miles from their home, the old families of Kukkunur assembled together with their peers from neighboring Kogali and also Masivadi

[[P498]] near distant Kolhapur, more than two hundred miles to the east.43 Amid a panoply of erotic apotropaic carvings, including what is probably the oldest instantiation of the infamous gadhegÄl of Maharashtra, where a donkey violates a woman, warning off those inclined to commit vandalism and theft, these networked collectivities played host to the representatives of the RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­a King Ká¹›á¹£á¹‡a III, the famed builder of the KailÄsa Temple at Ellora, whose daá¹‡á¸anÄyaka came to the region just for the occasion from the Koá¹…kana to honor Bagaliâ€™s Åšaiva bhaá¹­á¹­Äraka. Though the records are sadly damaged, Kukkunur seems to have received patronage from the RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­as since the time of Amoghavará¹£a I (800â€“878 CE). It was either in his reign or shortly thereafter that the surviving oldest temple complex in Kukkunur, the Navaliá¹…gÄ“Å›vara Temple, among the most important surviving examples of free standing RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­a temple architecture, was constructed. Thus, from the ninth to the middle of the thirteenth century, when the central ritual complex was abruptly dismantled and its officiants and inhabitants evicted, Kukkunur received unbroken patronage and frequent pilgrimage from across virtually the entire spectrum of the regional and imperial powers in the medieval Deccan. Just like we saw at Shirshangi, indeed using many of the same verses, Kukkunur is introduced through an extended series of pun laden stock phrases lauding the Kuntala country. It is beautiful like the locks of hair of a woman (kuntala). It is higher up on the body, and thus more exalted, than the middle region that is MadhyadeÅ›a or the girdle belt (kaÃ±cÄ«) that is Kancipuram. The district of BÄ•á¸·valavÄdi, in turn, is proclaimed to be the diadem that adorns

[[P499]] this precious lock of hair. Kukkunur is its crowning glory. In the midst of all this rote chancellery language, however, something of a more pointed message begins to assert itself as the Å›Äsanaâ€™s author begins to tell us some incongruous and unexpected things grounded in a decidedly ÅšÄkta worldview.

Previously, when the [goddess] â€œdestroyer of demonsâ€ grabbed and squeezed the Daityas with her hand, [the earth,] becoming full of the juice of their [daityasâ€™] fat, obtained the name MedinÄ« (endowed with fat). In the world, Kuntala country appears beautifully, like the beautiful hair of a woman from the BhÄrata country. The Kuntala country is like the hair of that woman. . . . It is to be cared for by the mahÄjanapadas, and it is an abode for garlands of sumanas flowers, and a lot of gold and jewels. The KuntaladÄ“Å›a alone, in which KÄá¸·ikÄ, JyÄ“á¹£á¹­hÄ•, and KapÄá¸·Ä«Å›a have taken up residence, is the best [place] among all the other countries. And it is because of their [i.e., KÄá¸·ikÄ, JyÄ“á¹£á¹­hÄ•, and KapÄá¸·Ä«Å›a] greatness that there is a foundation for the accumulation of merit in that place.44

Introducing for the first time the distinctive rhetorical register that animates this Å›Äsana, through lens of its epithet MedinÄ«â€”quite literally â€œthe fatty oneâ€â€”the earth and its origins are reimagined. In place of the expected story of the earth, originating when the boar incarnation of Viá¹£á¹‡u dredged up BhÅ«devÄ« out of the cosmic ocean, which had borne strong imperial implications since at least the time when this imagery was adopted by the Gupta kings as their royal emblem, our inscription tells a different story where royal power is entirely displaced by the goddess herself as rescue gives way to slaughter. This world, we are told, acquired its nature in the aftermath of the goddess taking some demons into her fists and crushing them into a pulp such that their vital juices dripped down and were absorbed by the world. The world, in other words, quite literally emerges from the palm of the goddessâ€™s hand and it is her power, inseparable from the aftermath of violent acts of sacrifice, that gives our reality its rasa.

[[P500]] Just as the earth became savory because the goddess slew some demons, in the process of imbuing it with new power, the KuntaladÄ“Å›a derives its fame and glory as the foremost place in the world because the very land has become imbued with a new kind of potency and promise. In material terms, the auspicious origins of this place have produced a land of great fecundity. Not only is this a place of considerable physical beauty, but the terrain abounds in gold, other precious metals, and jewels, a state of affairs with a number of practical consequences. Thus, the mountains in this region rank among the preferred mines of the CÄá¸·ukya empire, and the area abounds in goldsmiths, including some of the most respected master craftsmen patronized by the CÄá¸·ukyas. Indeed, several sites in the region, including Sudi, under the control of the administration of Kukkunur itself, functioned as the premiere mints for the production of coinage throughout the period, possessing their own distinctive dies and designs. For the same reasons, the soil itself has miraculous properties, for it is said to be so hyper fertile that it spontaneously renews itself with a fresh batch of new nutrients after each planting season in a manner that ensures that fields never have to lie fallow. Finally, on the social level, Kukkunur houses illustrious lines of pious BrÄhmaá¹‡as, and a hereditary administrative class of non- BrÄhmaá¹‡a landed farmers who serve as its mahÄjanas, all of whom operate under the direction of its long established line of illustrious and learned KÄlamukha ÄcÄryas.

As our text has begun to indicate, the source of all of this power and good fortune, which is transferred to those who take up practice in that place, is Kukkunur itself, because this is where the power of speechâ€”made manifest in an awesome and terrifying formâ€”to say nothing of a pantheon of mostly ferocious esoteric deities, have resided in splendor for many years, thriving under the care of yogins who call themselves KÄlamukhas. This state of affairs in and of itself is somewhat surprising, for recent scholarly literature has by and large placed

[[P501]] the KÄlamukhas at some distance from a recognizably Tantric milieu. During the twelfth century in particular, especially under the direction of the great KÄlamukha siddha Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara, whom we will encounter momentarily, through the production of a network of ritual centers, at Ron, and Sudi, and quite possibly at Gajendragad, the artisans and KÄlamukuha ÄcÄryas associated with Kukkunur extended their influence throughout the central Deccan. Apart from Kukkunur itself, the most spectacular of the nodes in this network today are certainly the ruins found at Sudi, which are comprised of three intact temples and a spacious step well. While in the tenth century the town seems to have maintained a special ritual relationship with the KÄpÄlikas of Laká¹£mÄ“Å›vara, due to a raid by the CÅá¸·a king, the social dynamics in the city were temporarily disrupted. Under the direction of Ä€hvahamalla/SomeÅ›vara I himself, the council of the town, whose kÄryacintakas were comprised of six gÄvuá¹‡á¸a lineages and eight sÄ•á¹­á¹­is, most likely in this case founding artisan families, had to be reinstated along with their samaya. At this point, either a different line of self-identified KÄlamukha ÄcÄryas moved in, or the older line in evidence from the tenth to the middle of the thirteenth century, when the land grant is abruptly discontinued and the inscriptional record suddenly stops, reimagined itself to suit the changing tenor of the times. Regardless, the governing institutional authorities on the ground remained constant, for the same original founding families are in evidence from

[[P502]] the tenth to the middle of the thirteenth century, when the land grant is abruptly discontinued, and the inscriptional record suddenly stops.

One of the most fascinating features of the Sudi corpus, perhaps not so surprising in light of what we have already seen of the regionâ€™s topography and resources, is the consistent reccurrence in its inscriptional record of artisans, especially goldsmiths and jewelers, many of whom also seem to have been serving as mahÄjanas. Thus, for example, in 1075, a jeweler by the name of MaddisÄ•á¹­á¹­i makes a series of donations to the AcalÄ“Å›vara temple.45 Likewise, in 1103, a key resident of the town, UttÅja, chief goldsmith to VikramÄditya VI himself, makes

Sudiâ€™s spectacular stepwell. Note that there are three alcoves for installing deities.

[[P503]] an offering of gold to the god Kammaá¹­Ä“Å›vara, the generic name for the kuladeva of the artisan castes who work with precious metals, who typically assumes the form of Bhairava. Indeed, the inscriptional record seems to suggest that during the late eleventh and early twelfth century Sudi served as an important site for the minting of gold coins.46 In functional terms, it was a central hub in the economic networks of circulation in the region. Should he be assigned by his superiors in the wider ranging monastic network to provide oversight to such a community, regardless of his personal interests and predilections, a particular KÄlamukha preceptor of necessity would have found himself operating not just as an initiatory guru to such communities, providing them with soteriological benefits, but also as a caste guru intimately involved in the business of his people. At Sudi, this would have likely entailed not only some direct involvement in both the pedagogy and production process associated with the specific artisanal crafts of the goldsmith and jewelers, especially in relation to objects produced for use in a temple context, but also in regard to the transactional economic activities that defined a community responsible for the production of legal tender as well as the determination of price floors and ceilings and general liquidity. In other words, no matter how learned he might be in Å›Ästra or exalted in his attainments from the successful execution of Tantric sÄdhanÄs, the professional life of a KÄlamukha ÄcÄrya abounded in banal responsibilities largely conditioned by the particular character of the places where he was posted and the sets of site-specific obligations which he has inherited.

At the same time, Sudi also seems to have been an important esoteric center. In particular, the temple now called Joá¸ukalaÅ›a, on account of its strange double domed structure connected by an internal maá¹‡á¸apa, which in the inscriptional record is repeatedly connected

[[P504]] both to the goldsmith and jeweler communities as well as the governing KÄlamukha authorities, seems to have played a key role in the esoteric ritual life of the region.

Sudi: Joá¸ukalaÅ›a temple exterior.

[[P505]] Though much of the exterior relief work is heavily eroded, as in the images on the left, which depicts Bhairava seated upon throne made from a serpent, or the one on the right, which seem to represent an introspective ÄcÄrya in a moment of relaxation, one can still see traces of a distinctively ÅšÄkta esoteric program. This impression is reinforced by the unique structure of the templeâ€™s two garbhagá¹›has, each of which presents us with a triad of alcoves clearly intended for housing an image for worship surrounding a central liá¹…ga. Our exploration of Kukkunur will offer us a tantalizing possibility for making sense of the triad. In particular,

[[P512]] While introducing the dÄna being offered by a regional nÄyaka and his horse trader uncle who is in charge of the accounts from Gujarat, the text here presents in sequence a ready taxonomy of Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s credentials. In the next verse, in contrast, effectively credentialing him in the role of Å›iká¹£Ä guru, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara is defined in terms of his scholastic accomplishments regarding â€œsecularâ€ mainstream Å›Ästric subjects of the sort that would prove pertinent to the education of a prince. In other words, insomuch as he is acting in a specific socially prescribed role, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varadeva is presented as perfectly instantiating the key dimensions of that role and nothing else. All of this stands in contradistinction to our usual presuppositions, namely, that evocations of the charismatic and magical abilities on the part of a certain special individual served the common-sense real-world function of conferring greater authority and thereby â€œlegitimatingâ€ religious actors. As we shall see, among his own close circle of devotees, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara is prized as an omniscient being with such enormous yogic attainments that he is capable of making reality itself bend to his will and

[[P513]] his whim. And yet, strangely enough, not a hint of these extraordinary capacities makes it onto his CV. Were we so inclined, we could now attend to a multitude of inscriptional sources from throughout the Deccan, and also from Orissa, that are concerned with rendering the professional lives of KÄlamukha preceptors in just this register. While all of these texts would benefit from this sort of close reading, the conventions of the genre render them less than ideal for furthering a more particularized understanding of the place that KÄlamukhas occupied in the religious imaginaries of the medieval world. What renders the Kukkunur inscription such an invaluable source for our understanding is that, rather unusually, in this text, the expressive professional register we have already been exploring operates in dynamic tension with a decidedly different way of talking about religion among the KÄlamukhas, one that offers the promise of effectively overturning the nascent consensuses emerging in our scholarly literature.

In emphatically labelling our KÄlamukha preceptor as ÅšivasiddhÄntÄ-cÄrya, SaiddhÄntika rÄjaguru and SaiddhÄntikarÄja our text would seem to be offering an almost painfully transparent account of Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s theological predilections. A person cursorily reading this text would be forgiven from concluding that, despite presiding over a goddess temple housing some deities with peculiar names, our KÄlamukhÄcÄrya was a staunch adherent to the doctrinal system of the Åšaiva SiddhÄnta. Often perceived to represent the ideological center at the heart of the Åšaiva Age (Sanderson 2009), by the twelfth century, the Åšaiva SiddhÄnta had evolved into a dualistic system, largely conforming to BrÄhmaá¹‡ical social norms, which proclaimed the existence of an ontological difference between the embodied human soul and the supreme lord. From this perspective, god, in the form SadÄÅ›iva, operates

[[P514]] in a pure realm estranged from materiality and involves himself in the functions of our sad fallen world only through intermediary powers. In our historiography, despite some evidence in the ritual literature itself for the cross-pollination of mantras and practices across communities, such religious formations are viewed as inviolable doctrinal unities that are both socially and doctrinally hostile to their proximate rivals. Reading further, however, the evidence on hand calls into question all such presuppositions:

Speaking of the comprehensiveness of his knowledge, there is no other siddhÄnta he does not know completely. There are no mantras he does not know in full. No Ä€gama remains unknown to him. Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara, it must be said, is veritable mine of esoteric wisdom.

Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara, the disciple of the wise, extracted (uddharisi) his definitive doctrine (siddhÄnta) from the Transmission of the Siddhas (SiddhÄvaá¸·i), the ÅšÄkta Transmission (DÄ“vyÄvaá¸·i), the SiddhÄnta Transmission (SÅmasiddhÄntÄvaá¸·i),50 and the Åšaiva Transmission (ÅšivÄgamÄvaá¸·i): there took place the abhiá¹£eka [of disciples] in that very place.51

In violation of our assumptions about the nature of KÄlamukha religious culture, which has until now, apart from contestations over the significance of the practice of bearing a skull ubiquitously associated with the community, been mostly shrouded in mystery, our text has unambiguously just shown us that Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara was actively engaged in the study and practice of the ÅšÄkta Tantras. He was not merely a grudging participant in such streams of esoteric knowledge in the service of professionalization, desperately struggling to keep up with the trends of the time. Rather, the evidence at hands demands that we envision him as a veritable Abhinavagupta of the Deccan, immersing himself in the study of the entire spectrum of esoteric knowledge, from the classical Åšaiva SiddhÄnta, to the newer revelations of the Kaula Siddha

[[P515]] lineages, the ÅšÄkta Tantras, and even the texts of the KÄpÄlikas, with the aim of crafting his own conclusive doctrinal position. In other words, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara is called the ÅšaivasiddhÄntikarÄja not because he follows Sadyojyotis, Bhaá¹­á¹­a RÄmakaá¹‡á¹­ha II, and the rest, but because he audaciously aspires to have his doctrine become the siddhÄnta, or settled position, that comprises Åšaiva knowledge. If this ambition is itself something new, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s allegiance to such systematicities seems to have been inherited through his lineage, which defined itself at the inception as â€œthe KÄlamukha order of the NakharÄ“Å›vara temple at Sudi, by whom the Ävaá¸·is are honored.â€ As our passages makes plain, the Ävaá¸·is in question are none other than SiddhÄvaá¸·i, DevyÄvaá¸·i, SiddhÄntÄvaá¸·i, and ÅšaivÄgamÄvaá¸·i. In other words, the KÄlamukhas at Sudi proudly proclaim that they practiced, preached, and transmitted the Tantric canon in all of its many forms. Sudi in turn, especially the eccentric second chamber at the Joá¸ukalaÅ›a temple, seems to have served as a venue for initiating their adepts. Such evidence, quite simply, has major consequences for the existing historiography on religion in the medieval Deccan.

In his seminal work The KÄpÄlikas and KÄlÄmukhas: Two Lost Åšaivite Sects, which to this day remains the only book that most scholars of Indian religions both in India and the West consult on the subject, David Lorenzen introduces his focus of study by observing quite candidly:

Judging from the large number of epigraphs recording donations to KÄlÄmukha temples and maá¹­has, these ascetics must have wielded considerable influence in the region. Unfortunately, few indications of their beliefs and ritual survive apart from the information which can be gleaned from these epigraphs. They reveal the existence of at least two major divisions of the KÄlÄmukha orderâ€”the Åšaktipariá¹£ad and the Siá¹ƒha- pariá¹£ad.52

[[P517]] There is much to commend about this pioneering work, which offers to date the only substantive survey of a portion of the relevant epigraphical archives offered from the perspective of a scholar of religion as well as some masterful mapping of specific preceptorial lineages across regional boundaries. Quite apart from only having access to a small fraction of the relevant materials, for in fact Lorenzen at every turn understates the ubiquity of KÄlamukha aligned institutions across the entire Deccan especially between the tenth and thirteenth centuries, Lorenzen labors under some presuppositions that do not serve his analysis. Like many authors, despite the fact that only an infinitesimal percentage of the corpus contains any specifications of vará¹‡a, by simple virtue of the fact that they are holding sacerdotal office, he simply assumes that all the KÄlamukha preceptors under discussion must be BrÄhmaá¹‡as. However, practically our only specific delineations of the caste identity of KÄlamukha gurus are found in inscriptions associated with the earlier records of the Siá¹ƒhapariá¹£ad. These are mostly located in Telangana and Andhra Pradesh on the edges of the KalyÄá¹‡i CÄá¸·ukya world. In other institutional contexts, in contrast, either the evidence mitigates against such conclusions or it is simply absent. Furthermore, the unstudied material culture related to these communities, as we shall see in the next chapter, effectively speaks for itself in outing the Tantric commitments of at least the Åšaktipariá¹£ad.

Such presuppositions lead Lorenzen to challenge as distortive polemic what is surely the most famous, and perhaps most accurate, synopsis of KÄlamukha social practices in Sanskrit discourse. This brief but important passage derives from the ÅšrÄ«vaiá¹£á¹‡ava ÄcÄrya RÄmÄnujaâ€™s discussion of the order in his ÅšrÄ«bhÄá¹£ya, which offers a commentary on the BrahmasÅ«trabhÄá¹£ya of BÄdarÄyaá¹‡a from an explicitly ÅšrÄ«vaiá¹£á¹‡ava perspective.

follows: the KÄpÄlikas, the KÄlÄmukhas, the PÄÅ›upatas, and the Åšaivas. [[P519]] Despite his disgust with their content, RÄmÄnuja, who apparently had access to doctrinal sources not accessible to us, has clearly done his homework. More generally speaking, RÄmÄnujaâ€™s understanding of KÄlamukha sociality is that it is founded on the theological presupposition that dÄ«ká¹£Ä liberates one not only from ontological bondage but from the strictures that inform the caste of oneâ€™s birth. If the social reality we are gradually reconstructing is a good fit with our documentary evidence, nevertheless, it poses serious problems for even the most learned scholarly treatments of the nature of the KÄlamukha traditions founded on a painstaking and ingenious reading of a deeply fragmentary textual record. Meditating [thus] he should wander day and night, ever mindful of the VyomavyÄpi mantra with its auxiliaries and Brahmas, eating little and full of resolution, indifferent to [extremes such as] heat and cold, ever benevolent and compassionate, contemplating, O best of sages, that the universe rests on his identity or that his identity pervades this universe, conquering his mind through yoga Nourished by the radiant power of his observance (caryÄtejopabá¹›mhitaá¸¥) the adept experiences the height of joy even as others verbally abuse or strike him. He should look on the whole world as his kin, thinking that nobody is either better or worse than himself. By manifesting this behaviour he experiences an inner joy.60

In his analysis Sanderson is primarily concerned with documenting the many continuities between this mode of religiosity and the more familiar AtimÄrga practices of the PÄÃ±cÄrthika PÄÅ›upatas, such as the LÄkulas â€œcontriving to rouse the hatred of the worldâ€ by feigning misconduct in the manner of the third stage of PÄÅ›upata practice about which so much has been written. He is also intent on connecting LÄkula ritual practices to the elaborate taxonomies of distinctive Åšaiva cosmologies recoverable, if often in residual forms, from a close reading of Tantric chapters of the ontological hierarchies that make up the many subtle worlds that overlap with our own. Indeed, it is on this basis that he is able to confirm that one of what Lorenzen has identified as among our earliest explicitly KÄlamukha inscriptions contains a veiled reference to the scriptural canon and taxonomies that the Åšaiva Ä€gamas treat as the defining resources for the LÄkula tradition. And it is on this basis that Sanderson suggests that, though he seems not fully aware of the extent of this phenomenon, the predominant

[[P523]] religious community in medieval Deccan were AtimÄrga adepts adhering to a lost canon of scriptures, whose core theology involved a gnostic style ascent into the higher realms as well as a radical renunciation of all forms of action as the culminating phase of spiritual practice.

In light of our own explorations thus far, however, what should be equally evident is how frequently the two specialized vows associated with ascetics who have taken up the skull, about which Sanderson has written with such insight and eloquence, also make reference to an assumed body of devotional values and social comportments shared by all rudrabhaktas that have their point of origin in â€œÅšivaÅ›Ästrasâ€: in other words, in the Åšivadharma and its successors. As RÄmÄnuja tells us, KÄlamukha culture seems to have been comprised of at least two components. First, there is ritual inclusion in the community as a non-ascetic, an act which entails the transmutation of the original caste of the initiate into that of a BrÄhmaá¹‡a. Secondarily, as we have seen, one can become a yati through assuming a skull vow, which seemingly must have entailed the types of practices about which Sanderson has written with such clarity.

As straightforward a formulation as this might seems to us, however, it poses seriously complications for the criteria that scholarship has used for identifying the features that differentiate the AtimÄrga from MantramÄrga traditions. In what is probably the most recent distillation of cutting-edge scholarship on the subject, for example, in an essay contained in the Continuum Companion to Hindu Studies, Dominic Goodall and Harunaga Isaacson have argued:

We have spoken of â€˜continuityâ€™, but what characterizes the shift from AtimÄrga to MantramÄrga? How does the latter set itself apart? The new Tantric religion for the first time placed strong (perhaps equal) emphasis on two goals: liberation (mukti) and the enjoyment of supernatural powers (bhukti). Both goals were attained through the power of spells (vidyÄ, mantra), the propitiation and use of which required a ritual

[[P524]]

technology of considerable complexity and both required initiation (dÄ«ká¹£Ä). Now there may seem to be nothing very new here: initiation is required for the AtimÄrga too. . . . But these elements have been reconfigured and, in some cases, reinterpreted: the spells of the MantramÄrga are with five exceptions (the five brahmamantras) not Vedic, and initiation (dÄ«ká¹£Ä) is no longer simple a necessary rite of entrance into a new religion, as it has been for at least the PÄÃ±cÄrthika PÄÅ›upatas, but has become instead a transformative rite. Liberation is no longer brought about, as in the AtimÄrga, through a lengthy progression of post-initiatory practice, but is conceived of as being essentially the result of cutting the bonds by Åšiva, acting through the guru and with mantras as his instruments, at the time of initiation. Furthermore, this liberation, conceived of both as release from suffering and at the same time as the realization of omniscience and omnipotence, was offered not only to brahmin males, as in the AtimÄrga, but to those of all vará¹‡as and, in some cases, both sexes.61

Quite simply, the evidence at hand calls into question of the distinctiveness of nearly every feature that Goodall and Isaacson have invoked as distinguishing the AtimÄrga. Working in reverse order, as we have seen, KÄlamukuha communities are identified with the initiation of all vará¹‡as and castes, a process that transmutes the adept into a BrÄhmaá¹‡a. In this sense, at least, for them also, dÄ«ká¹£Ä was understood as a transformative rite and not merely a rite of passage. Quite apart from the brahmamantras, our KÄlamukhas make open use of non-Vedic mantras, especially the vyomavyÄpin system, which the SiddhÄnta appropriates. Finally, as we will soon explore, as part of their professional duties, KÄlamukhÄcÄryas were intimately involved in magical practices both on their own behalf and in the service of various regional powers. In fact, practically the only detail left standing is the supposedly â€œgradualistâ€ nature of AtimÄrga soteriology, as we have little evidence relating to such matters preserved in our sources. This is not to say the historical development of traditions that Goodall, Isaacson, and Sanderson have proposed is necessarily incorrect. Indeed, as Sanderson has noticed, some of

[[P525]] the earliest strata of our inscriptional record concerning the KÄlamukhas seemingly refer directly to the lost canon of PramÄá¹‡aÅ›Ästras that served as foundational LÄkula scriptures. It may well be the case that, in the mid-eighth century or earlier, the tradition was aligned with distinctively LÄkula doctrinal formulations, exclusively initiated BrÄhmaá¹‡as, primarily made use of Vedic mantras, and was solely oriented towards soteriology. But traditions and their institutions do not merely fossilize at their point of origin, and by at least the middle of the tenth century, there are reasons to believe that, especially in greater Karnataka, the people calling themselves KÄlamukhas are committed to very different values and practices. Indeed, by the eleventh century, there are strong indications that KÄlamukha maá¹­has throughout the Deccan have become the primary sites for the practice of Tantric ÅšÄkta rituals and the circulation of these scriptures. While the archive associated with Kukkunur transparently communicates this state of affairs, in broader terms, the material cultural record requests that such a reorientation was not only confined to the KÄlamukhas based out of Sudi, but was also at work within the Åšaktipariá¹£ad in general, to say nothing of the myriad of other localized KÄlamukha lineages attested to in our inscriptional record that our scholarship has scarcely noticed. Quite apart from its thick description of the Tantric pantheons that called the place home, what is particularly striking about the archive at Kukkunur is that it paints a vivid portrait of the interplay between the quest for worldly power or supernatural attainments and more soteriological aims. In hinting at how a single institutionâ€”indeed, a single personâ€”adapted itself to accommodate both these aspects of human longing for measure of transcendence, it perhaps offers us glimpses of the process whereby the LÄkula tradition merged into the streams of the Tantras.

Quite apart from its poetic flourish, for our purposes, this astonishing passage is doing two rather distinctive sorts of rhetorical work. At the most obvious level, it begins the process

[[P533]] of â€œweaponizingâ€ the core tenets of Trika theology. In the simplest terms, the classical tradition imagines the universe as utterly pervaded by the garland of letters that make up the Sanskrit alphabet, which are none other than the goddess SiddhayogeÅ›varÄ« herself. The types of consummate bibliophiles and spiritual enthusiasts that tend to be inclined to study and write about the Trika and its Å›Ästras, especially reading through the lens provided by the writings of Abhinavagupta, often perceive this hyper-literary vision of a world permeated and made beautiful by words in borderline utopian terms. In this regard, they offer a strong misreading of tradition. If, from the perspective of a commentator like BhÄskara when he writes on the ÅšivasÅ«tras, the permeation of the world by semantic and phonemic entities who are nothing other than the webs of goddesses under the control of the mistress of language offers an almost dystopian vision of how language constrains human freedoms, in the vernacular Trika- inflected text we have before us this same conceptual framework is redeployed in the service of quotidian opportunism. Quite simply, since the goddess is already â€œin everythingâ€ in the form of speech, to worship speech is synonymous with the worship of sovereignty over all living beings. Conversely, the censure of speech and the goddess that embodies it intrinsically renders a person powerless. These themes that will be made more clear in subsequent verses.

At the very same time our text gestures in the direction of lofty esoteric understandings bound up in a philosophy of language at least somewhat indebted to Bhartá¹›hari, it simultaneously is addressing itself to a very different social register, that of pan-regional â€œvernacularâ€ religious traditions whose textual foundations elude us, if they ever existed. Already in the seventh century, when the Pallavas build the KailÄsanÄtha Temple at Kanchipuram, worship of a seemingly non-Tantric mother goddess commonly referred to as Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ was so widespread in the Tamil country that these mainline initiates into the Åšaiva

[[P534]] SiddhÄnta felt the need to incorporate a portrait of this deity, depicted as a portly, possibly pregnant, middle-aged squatting female figure, onto the wall of their royal temple alongside what is otherwise a purely PurÄá¹‡ic pantheon.

Around this same time, the worship of this goddess spreads into Southern Karnataka and from there into the western Deccan, where it undergoes some further transformations, resulting in two core iconographic variants. Gradually, the squatting female figure becomes younger and more alluring, along the way acquiring a crow as her emblem. As one approaches the borders of Maharashtra, in contrast, prefiguring her early modern recasting as the goddess of misfortune, Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄâ€™s crow is replaced by a donkey, she picks up a winnowing fan or broom, and, hinting at her assimilation into the cremation ground-tinged world of the ÅšÄkta Tantras, her companions are transmuted into ghouls and vetÄlas. Perhaps it is appropriate that at a place like Kukkunur, situated between these two geographical and culture domains, Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ seems to have embodied an amalgamation of these currents, for our text mentions the killing beak of her terrible crow, which pecks the rogue to death right after he has been struck by her winnowing fan.

Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ as she appears, respectively, throughout south India and in borderlands of the Marathawada

Indeed, the very next verse seems to provide further support for such a reading. Here once again, the esoteric goddess at the center of the system is identified with the other major trans- regional goddess worshipped throughout the Deccan and Tamil country: Mother MÄri,

[[P538]] Despite our textâ€™s aspirations to resembling the phonic texture and easeful word play of old Kannada kÄvya, at the level of semantics, the sensibility on display here, apart from the concluding verse, is much more akin to what we would expect from â€œfolkâ€ religion. JyÄ“á¹£á¹­hÄ is depicted as neither particularly good or nor particularly bad but simply unimaginably powerful and somewhat capricious. As opposed to her power over language being rendered in lofty philosophical terms, as often the case in the Trika Tantras, where she is the fundamental ground of consciousness that animates the conceptual and emotional processes within humans and other beings, here her control over the power of speech and cognition operates in much more gross terms. She controls men and demons alike, either through the power of her mÄyÄ, which causes them to become deluded and forget things, or, rather charmingly, by quite literally climbing on top their tongues, so that they are forced to misspeak. This, it seems, is what happened to the gargantuan demon Kumbhakará¹‡a, the elder brother of RÄvaá¹‡a, renowned just as much for his marathon naps as he was for his formidable physical strength. He made his initial entreaty to the gods, and not to JyÄ“á¹£á¹­hÄ, and so the boon he received was sleep (nidra) instead of the destruction of the gods (nirddÄ•va) that he had originally intended. In contrast, we are told, anyone who pays the goddess proper homage receives her favor, all of which seems to take the form of quotidian things such as the destruction of enemies, wealth, or political power. Woven into this celebration of Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s many virtues and talents, which, in their richness exceed the formal conventions of professional self-presentation, we find what is
