In his recent dissertation, Ben Williams has helpfully identified a reconceptualization of the nature â€œTantric authority,â€ first evident in the late ÅšÄkta Tantras themselves and then in the Kaula and siddha traditions that inform the Kashmiri Trika exegetes. In the initial phase, virtually for the first time, the descent and transmission of specific forms of Tantric knowledge is ascribed to individualâ€”indeed, individuatedâ€”at least nominally human agents, such as MatsyendranÄtha. In the second, in what Williams has referred to as representing the â€œmature Kaula idiom of religious authority,â€

Each individual guru in the lineage is not just a faithful transmitter of the letter of scripture that extrinsically governs their religious lives. More precisely, they are tasked with â€œreenactingâ€ the revelatory truths in the form of a â€œsudden unfoldingâ€ in the â€œvisionary consciousnessâ€ of their â€œown inner reality.â€ Therefore, the exercise of religious authority is now dynamically rooted in the divine identity of the individual Siddha master. This model of revelation helps explain an extraordinary feature in writings of post-scriptural authors in the Krama, namely their â€œreadiness to innovate.â€ The words inspired by the inner realization of these Siddhas are tantamount to scripture, and no longer need to conform strictly to the fixed doctrine of an external scriptural canon.73

[[P547]] Williamâ€™s focus is primarily the multigenerational revelations of the teachers of the Kashmiri Krama, who, beginning with JÃ±ÄnanetranÄtha, pen self-ascribed works of revelation that incorporate effusive accounts of the circumstances surrounding their own spiritual experiences. Williams argues that these are then redeployed in the lineage pedagogically as skillful means for facilitating in future generation of disciples the mimetic â€œre-experienceâ€ of the processes that brought about the liberation of the original revealers. Williamâ€™s major point is that this reconceptualization of the nature of religious authority as grounded in the direct experience of liberated ÅšÄkta gurus makes possible a sea-change in values through which the authoritative word of a liberated guru replaces revealed scripture as the primary source of religious guidance. As he convincingly demonstrates, it is this sort of conceptualization that informs Abhinavaguptaâ€™s core interpretive project in the TantrÄloka, a magisterial work that in essence sets out to reconceptualize and in a sense replace the diverse Tantric canons the great siddha polymath has set out to synthesize. In this respect, it is not Tantra but second order TantraÅ›Ästras, like the TantrÄloka, that are seen as providing the most effective and informed vehicle for liberating bound souls of various natures and predilections. As Williams demonstrates, throughout his writings, Abhinavagupta repeatedly invokes his own experiences and spiritual autobiography as proof of the authenticity and efficacy of the systems of practice, understanding and belief that he presents. Indeed, if I understand him correctly, Williams seems to suggest that the rhetorical structure of the TantrÄloka as a whole is deeply informed by this distinctly Kaula post-scriptural model of charismatic authority.

Williamsâ€™s model provides a useful point of reference not only in terms of the real continuities we see between his sources and our own text, and what further light this sheds on the religious ecologies of the medieval Deccan, but also in terms of bringing into sharper relief

[[P548]] the substantial divergences between the two discursive worlds. In many ways, the parallels are uncanny. Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara, as we have already seen, in a manner eerily akin to Abhinavaguptaâ€™s project in the TantrÄloka, â€œextracted (uddharisi) his definitive doctrine (siddhÄnta) from the Transmission of the Siddhas (SiddhÄvaá¸·i), the ÅšÄkta Transmission (DevyÄvaá¸·i), the SiddhÄnta Transmission (SomasiddhÄntÄvaá¸·i), and the Åšaiva Transmission (ÅšivÄgamÄvaá¸·i),â€ and then initiated his disciples into the resulting new system of praxis. In as much as he oversees a ritual center dedicated to SiddhayogeÅ›varÄ«, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara is also, of sorts, a master of the Trika. Within the context of the distinctively divergently textured small selection of verses we have just been exploring, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara is represented not just as being a perfect conduit for existing traditions but as a source of revelation in his own right, a liberated being whose every action instantiates and communicates spiritual insight. Just as Williams writes that â€œhis words are tantamount to scripture,â€ our text proclaims, â€œwhatever he taught, that was Tantra.â€ Such similarities are not circumstantial. Our text itself asserts quite baldly that Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara has studied the siddha transmissions and made use of them in implementing his doctrine, and that he has done so in a place specifically dedicated to the types of cremation ground-tinged sexual encounters with the yoginÄ«s that also lie at the heart of the scriptural tradition of the KÄlÄ« Krama. And indeed, the evidence cementing such a connection has been hiding right in front of us all this time. Unlike some other lines that bear initiatory names such as -Å›akti and -rÄÅ›i, like all of the KÄlamukhas in his lineage, and indeed in many others, Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s last name ends in ÄªÅ›vara. As we will see when we consult some of the Tantric scriptural sources that were used at Kukkunur in chapter 6, in this register of discourse, x-Ä«Å›vara and x-nÄtha are used interchangeably. Indeed, had chancellery conventions deemed it more appropriate to systematically make use of the other monikerâ€”in other words, had we began our analysis

[[P549]] talking about Kaá¸·anÄtha overseeing the temple of SiddhayogeÅ›varÄ«â€”much of the materials we have explored in this chapter would seem much less jarring. For here and there, one can discern echoes of this sort of understanding, or at least formative antecedents to what will become core tropes of post-Tantric esoteric yogin traditions. After all, is not a verse such as â€œOne who is not devoted to her alone, says something like â€œJyÄ“á¹£á¹­hÄ, what kind of goddess is that?â€ What god can such a person take refuge with? He cannot save his own life. . . . All that being said, there are equally substantive differences that demand our attention. Most notably, invocations of charismatic and individual centered authority presented in the two different discourses perform essentially the opposite kind of rhetorical work. As Williams has demonstrated, in the writings of Abhinavagupta, the â€œmature Kaula idiom of religious authority,â€ the religious individual attainment of an omniscient Siddha, along with a detailed autobiographical account of his preceptorial line, becomes the primary means of authenticating a composition and situating its relative authority within a scale of other texts. In contrast, in violation of the typical expectations of scholars of religion, in the Kukkunur Å›Äsana,

[[P550]] Kaá¸·Ä“Å›varaâ€™s status as an omniscient being and sui generis source of revelation is not only never invoked as a means of further legitimating his authority, but it is actually actively excluded from discussions of his credentials and professional career as an esteemed religious authority. As we have seen, our text is almost obsessed with delineating chains and sources of authority. Virtually all of these, however, are institutionally derived, the product of holding co- currently a series of increasingly exalted offices that themselves confer connections to specific types of political and religious actors. Just as significantly, with one important exception, which will be the focus of chapter 6, these offices are not primarily defined in specifically doctrinal terms. Or rather, one might say that in many cases, Tantras were something that were â€œlearned on the job,â€ with the question of which Tantras being less a matter of personal interest than of professional necessity. In other words, quite apart from the fact that the marvelous texts of one of them have survived while the otherâ€™s teachings, if they were ever written down, have not, perhaps the key distinction between a Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara and Abhinavagupta is that Abhinavagupta did not have to hold down a day job. More than an amusingly flippant remark, it is a compelling suggestion that the radically different texture of the Tantric exegetical tradition as they manifest in Kashmir and the much less well understood, understudied and yet unquestionably more influential Tantric socio-imaginaries that dominated the medieval Deccan have their roots precisely in this sociological difference. As far as we can tell, figures like SomÄnanda and Bhaá¹­á¹­a RÄmakaá¹‡á¹­ha II did not have to run a bank, preside over huge public celebrations on a regular basis, travel between the hundreds of different branches of their monastery, or be forced into a crash course in the particular paddhati associated with a new shrine when they suddenly got a â€œpromotion.â€ Nor, from what we can see of religion in Kashmir, were they juridically and liturgically responsible for overseeing the ritual and pragmatic needs of the

[[P551]] huge internally differentiated publics that inhabited the dozens if not hundreds of villages incorporated within their domain. Perhaps most fundamentally, as the patterns of citation in the TantrÄloka demonstrate again and again, none of the people through whose writings we have learned most of what we think we know about Tantra occupied a position where they were responsible for overseeing the religious activities centered around a large temple complex. Most pointedly, none of them served as caste gurus for the communities of artisans that built such temples. Unlike Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara, none of them, in other words served as sthÄnÄcÄryas, harnessing their spiritual power to bring icons to life alongside teams of professional masons, metal smelters, wood carvers, and stone sculptors. The often indeterminate picture we have of KÄlamukha culture stems in large part from our failures to consider these facets of the MantramÄrga traditions, its mediation through institutional as opposed to primarily doctrinal frames, and the myriad of connections that exist between ritual technology and â€œtechnologyâ€ in the more basic sense of specialized technical knowledge that makes possible the production of specific forms of material culture around which then emerge specific modes of sociality. In beginning to tell that story, we can gradually grapple with how the lineages that emerge out of the AtimÄrga became the core vehicles for the transmission of the MantramÄrga. We can begin to make sense of why it is the case that, by the middle of twelfth century in the medieval Deccan, when we hear a KÄlamukha speak, everything he says â€œbecomes mantra.â€

Åšivadharma Trumps RÄjadharma: The CÄá¸·ukya Kings of Kalyana in Their Own Words

As Murari Lal Nagar notes in passing in the introduction to his edition of SomeÅ›vara IIIâ€™s mahÄkÄvya, â€œThe Rise of Vikramaâ€ (VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya), in the process of telling the very same story, SomeÅ›varaâ€™s text draws so heavily upon the tropological reserves of his

[[P552]] predecessor, the great Kashmiri poet Bilhaá¹‡a, that his prose at times approaches paraphrase.75 And yet, to speak the princeâ€™s work as either â€œderivativeâ€ or offering merely a corrective political apologetics to the Kashmiri poetâ€™s subtle and subversive vision does little justice to SomeÅ›varaâ€™s own considerable literary and intellectual accomplishments. Furthermore, it entirely overlooks the fundamental dissonance between the two poetâ€™s worldviews. If Yigal Bronner has represented Bilhaá¹‡aâ€™s poetics as expressing a â€œdefiant antilocal tone,â€ that sets out to misprision76 â€œan environment devoid of cultural refinementâ€ into the proper (Sanskrit) cosmopolitan idiom palatable to an urbane Kashmiri, SomeÅ›vara demands to be read as offering an unabashedly localizing literary language. Whereas the intellectual project of the Sanskrit cosmopolis repopulates the world with Indiaâ€™s answer to Weberian ideal typesâ€”for what else are the nÄyaka, nÄyikÄ, and the restâ€”in rendering a hyper-particularized textured world in miniature, SomeÅ›varaâ€™s art is almost anthropological. In short, as we shall see, to the delight of the social historian of religion, what the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhudaya offers us is a â€œview from somewhere,â€ and that somewhere is unmistakably the medieval Deccan.

The surviving portion of the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya is mostly dedicated to telling the story of the circumstances surrounding the conception and birth of the children of the CÄá¸·ukya king SomeÅ›vara I, commonly called Ä€havamalla (1042â€“1068 CE). In other words, SomeÅ›vara III takes on the task of writing about his own grandfather in the prime of his life as well as the prepubescent escapades of his own father and uncle. In doing so, our author takes over eighty pages of dense Sanskrit prose to cover the same ground that Bilhaá¹‡a dispensed in his first two

[[P553]] cantos in barely two hundred verses. The plot is very simple. Despite his royal prowess and almost overly active libido, Ä€havamalla and his wife failed to produce a son and heir, a fact that brought them both great grief. Like so many desperate couples, the CÄá¸·ukya king and his wife seek out fertility counselling. This being medieval India, the guidance he receives, from the finest BrÄhmaá¹‡a ritualists in the land, takes the form of a ritual program entailing sponsoring and overseeing the performance of the sixteen mahÄdÄnas drawn from the classical DharmaÅ›Ästras. Against all expectations, the meticulous execution of SmÄrta ritual, of a sort, results in the production, not of an ideal heir, but rather of a monster in human form. It is only when, abandoning his learned councilors, Ä€havamalla himself sets out on pilgrimage to Srisailam and performs a regiment of grueling austerities, that the conception of the real king, the great VikramÄditya VI, becomes possible.

As many scholars would be inclined, it is not entirely unfair to read a story like this as an exercise in justifying the contested succession to the CÄá¸·ukya throne, a â€œjust so storyâ€ about why, in defiance of the normative standards of primogeniture, it was right for the great VikramÄditya VI to rebel against his elder brother, kill him, and then take the throne. But, as is so often the case with kÄvya, the plot of the poem and the work the poem sets out to perform are two very different things. In his masterful essay on the VikramÄá¹…kadevacarita, which also offers the only recent scholarly treatment of SomeÅ›varaâ€™s text, Lawrence McCrea has argued that Bilhaá¹‡aâ€™s poem offers a meditation on the inherently morally reprehensible nature of royal power that concludes in rejecting â€œthe very notion that kingly virtue can be meaningfully said to exist at all, except as a kind of public relations ploy.â€77 Bilhaá¹‡aâ€™s point, as McCrea tells us, is that it is not the substance of the world, but the creative power of the poet, that creates value.

[[P554]] Virtue and character are acts of the imagination made real through aestheticized speech. Projected onto the blank canvas of lived reality, through his craft, the poet makes a world that is more real than the real. It is the evaluations and judgements embedded in his literary vision that becomes reality for posterity. As McCrea so aptly puts it, â€œBilhaá¹‡aâ€™s claim here, if taken seriously, implies that it is poetry, and poetry alone, that lies at the root of our moral evaluation of figures such as RÄma and RÄvaá¹‡a. The fact that we admire RÄma and revile RÄvaá¹‡a is solely due to the power of VÄlmÄ«kiâ€™s great poem. If only RÄvaá¹‡a had had the foresight to employ VÄlmÄ«ki himself, the status of the two could just as well be reversed, and we would all be admonished to â€˜Act like RÄvana, not like RÄma.â€™â€78 As a careful reading will soon make evident, SomeÅ›varaâ€™s VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya offers a systematic repudiation of this worldview and the variety of relativistic social constructionism that gives it intellectual substance. Virtue, SomeÅ›vara tells us, is very much a product of circumstance, and yet it is still very real and operates a priori to the professionalized practices of hired literati. Inspiration and greatness, he sets out to show us, are not the exclusive property of the poets of proper pedigree. On the part of a faithful king, wise enough to reject the many intermediaries that attach themselves like barnacles onto his office, these capacities can be received directly from the gods. While Bilhaá¹‡a is wary of and disgusted by the practice of royal power, SomeÅ›vara III is skeptical and disenchanted regarding the adequacy of BrÄhmaá¹‡ical normativity. His poem demands to be read as repeatedly asserting that its maps do not correspond to CÄá¸·ukya territory and towards this end he consistently replaces the norms of the imagined universe of the Sanskrit cosmopolis with the variegated messy realities of the Deccan, teaming with competing life-worlds, of the sort we have already seen in contemporary inscriptions as well as within the Tantras. In other

[[P555]] words, despite the fact that the CÄá¸·ukya emperor is the well sired heir to the most powerful throne in the Deccan, and our old friend HabbÄ•ya NÄyaka, the local ÅšÅ«dra big man with whom we began this chapter, most certainly was not, in many ways the two men were inhabitants of the same episteme, the contours of which have until now evaded the learned maps of the modern scholarly imaginaries.

After some brief and now fragmentary verses, the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya begins with a poetic tableau of the land, the waters, and the forests of Karnataka. [[P557]] In this astonishing and peculiar passage, the typical stereotypes of the normative vará¹‡a systemâ€”BrÄhmaá¹‡as do ritual, Ká¹£atriyas fight, VaiÅ›yas trade, ÅšÅ«dras serveâ€”seamlessly fuse together with an awareness of the realities on the ground during the medieval period, providing our author with occasion for social commentary and even observational commedia. When SomeÅ›vara talks about BrÄhmaá¹‡as, he talks about them both on their own terms, as embodiments of Vaidika knowledge, while also suggesting the popular consciousness, evident even today in the rural Deccan, that ordinary people value them because they think their rites bring the rains. Speaking of Ká¹£atriyas, our author nods to their status as a class of warrior patrons, and then brings out the more pertinent social facts that their ranks are comprised of the folks the CÄá¸·ukyas have defeated in battle such that their primary identity has become their function as vassals. SomeÅ›vara is a keen and irreverent observer of the transactionalities of daily life, and thus readily admits to prevalence of financial fraud within his kingdom. Along similar lines, attending to actualities instead of ideals, our author is well aware that in a world teaming with zamindar bhaá¸·Äras with hundreds if not thousands of small farmers and slaves under their direction, multicaste merchant guilds, and empowered vÄ«ras like HabbÄ•ya, it would be risible to represent the ÅšÅ«dra as just comprising a servant class. Much more fundamentally and audaciously, as you can see in the above passage, SomeÅ›vara begins his account of â€œwho are the peopleâ€ by drastically changing the scope of the category of vará¹‡a itself. Finally, and perhaps most problematically from our perspective, SomeÅ›vara IIIâ€™s account of vará¹‡a incorporates into the fold the people we call Dalits. As our author is at pains to make clear, even in its expanded form, the system of vará¹‡a is completely inadequate for encompassing the scope of lived human reality. [[P561]] From the perspective of our author, it is only fitting that this place, Kará¹‡Ätaka, which excels all other places in the world, would leave great writers groping for the sort of language and idiom that would capture its essence. And in a sense, the questions he poses, â€œvyÄvará¹‡yaá¸¥ kair na kará¹‡Äá¹­adeÅ›aá¸¥,â€ offers its own answer, for not only is this very strange Sanskrit, but, as we will see repeatedly, rhetorical ejaculations of this sort form the structural glue that holds the descriptive portions of this prose kÄvya together. The typical structure is to introduce a topic, offer many self-contained pen portraits illustrating different aspects of the subject, and then to build to crescendo that culminates in a parenthetical aside along the lines of â€œwho could describe that,â€ or â€œwhat more is there to say,â€ or â€œwhat cannot be done [given the aforementioned conditions we just been discussing],â€ immediately after which there is shift of subject, focus, or sub-section. While the nature of Sanskrit prose remains notoriously understudied and undertheorized, for apart from Yigal Bronnerâ€™s recent work on Å›leá¹£a and â€œvery long compounds,â€ Robert Hueckstedtâ€™s 1985 monograph The Style of BÄá¹‡a: An Introduction to Sanskrit Prose Poetry remains the only study of this sort,85 even a cursory engagement with pre-thirteenth century poetic Sanskrit prose reveals how jarringly out of step SomeÅ›vara style is from the literary canons of â€œgoodâ€ Sanskrit prose kÄvya. Succinctly, one can search high and low but neither BÄá¹‡a nor DhanapÄla ever writes quite like this. In fact, however, as you might have recognized, we have actually seen these rhetorical choices before. For what is â€œsa vyÄvará¹‡yaá¸¥ kair na kará¹‡ÄtadeÅ›aá¸¥,â€ â€œwho can possibly describe the Kará¹‡ata countryâ€? Is it not in effect an act of back translation of a line analogous to â€œÄr baá¹‡nisar kukkanÅ«raá¹ƒ,â€ â€œwho can possibly describe Kukkunur!â€ such as we found in the Kaá¸·Ä“Å›vara

[[P562]] inscription? Indeed, is not this whole verse a linguistic transposition of a most familiar rhetoric, â€œdeÅ›aá¹…gaá¸·Åá¸·agÄ• kuntaá¸·a deÅ›amÄ• migiá¸·,â€ among all the other countries the KuntaladÄ“Å›a is the bestâ€? As we have seen time and again, much of this language is rooted in chancellery formulas that are refitted and repurposed to meet the needs of a range of circumstances. In short, SomeÅ›vara III has adopted an unabashedly localizing literary language such that Sanskrit itself is made Kannadiga, and Sanskrit kÄvya ceases to be an insular unmarked world onto itself and instead, notwithstanding its non-Jaina commitments, sits comfortably aside Ranna and the rest of the great old poets.

In place of the rhetorical invocation of the comprehensive and peerless nature of a great society, founded on a restricted tropological canon of the sort that is common to kÄvya, SomeÅ›vara revels in this recounting of actual human diversity in miniature, including in his presentation, as our translation alas cannot, a whole host of identities and their associated paraphernalia that elude the grasp of our dictionaries. Returning to our text, this quintessentially medieval Deccani celebration of diversity in social function is paired with a measured recognition of the necessity of religious difference that is also very much of its time. [The temples of KalyÄá¹‡a can be described:]

Succinctly, SomeÅ›vara takes advantage of a long series of nested clauses dedicated to describing the sacred sites in his capital to try his hand at open mike night comedyâ€”with mixed success. Some of these puns make you wince. A few of SomeÅ›varaâ€™s jokes, such as the last one about the Buddhists, are actually funny. Regardless, two features of this discourse are well worth attending to. [[P566]] Having set the scene, our author now introduces us to the line of our protagonists, the KalyÄá¹‡i CÄá¸·ukyas themselves. Indeed, with a few words about the well-known ancient stories of the royal line of the line, SomeÅ›vara dispenses with all but a cursory attempt of making sense of the deep history of his lineage. SomeÅ›varaâ€™s objective, rather straight forwardly, is to make it clear that his KalyÄá¹‡i CÄá¸·ukyas represent a direct continuation of the original Caá¸·ukyas, who from the seventh century onward ruled at Badami. And rather than being intrinsically fit for rule, in SomeÅ›varaâ€™s account, periodically throughout the generations, the splendor of the line is renewed through a direct

Interesting enough, the identity of the acting deity itself varies considerably such that, while SatyÄÅ›raya himself is said to have received the favor of Viá¹£á¹‡u (viá¹£á¹‡uprasÄda), PulakeÅ›in, we are told, had contact with VÄ«ralaká¹£mÄ« (vÄ«ralaká¹£mÄ«samparka) and his great grandson in turn received the favor of an unspecified BhagavatÄ« from worshipping the pair of her sandals.

SomeÅ›varaâ€™s narrative collapses sacred and historical time, reading human political history as the direct continuation of the unending story of how god periodically comes down into the world to destroy evils that proved too much for human beings to handle. Indeed, the conceptual framing at hand prefigures nearly everything that our literature has ascribed to the cultural encounter between the people who would come to be called Hindus and their Muslim rulers, suggesting perhaps that, regardless of their theological predilections and ethnic identities, this was merely the common idiom for talking about oneâ€™s political rivals. Here indeed the role of pratinÄyaka in this cosmic dharma is played by the poor RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­as, by all accounts a perfectly pakka early medieval dynastic power singularly unsuited to the role, who had reigned on and off for past five hundred years, providing copious patronage throughout the period for Vadika ritual sacrifices. Nevertheless, to fit the rhetoric, through an act of quite literal demonization, they are pigeonholed into the role of the unrighteous rulers who oppress the people and burden the earth and thus must be eradicated. Just as he came before in the form

[[P569]] of the Vaiá¹£á¹‡ava avatÄras, NÄrÄyaá¹‡a must descend again into the world, this time taking on the guise of the CÄá¸·ukya king. Having assented to this task, we leave the company of the gods as the text aptly causes its reader to experience their own sort of descent back into the mundane world. This whole prophetic vision, we suddenly discover, is in fact an auspicious dream in the mind of the rÄjapurohita that he relates to the parents of the future Tailapa.91 Indeed, once it is clear to the reader that divine sanction has been given for the task of overthrowing the RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­a imperium, our author does not bother to spell out the details of how they were overthrown; we simply learn that they were, and the texture of the text regresses back to fleshing out the crib notes on the royal panegyrics. Rather pointedly, the account that follows of the most recent CÄá¸·ukya kings simply elides most of the golden age of Kannada literature, which mostly takes places under the next four rulers. It is as if history, mapped onto the punctuated moments of divine intervention, begins with Tailapa and then begins again with SomeÅ›vara IIIâ€™s own grandfather.

Thus, while we might anticipate that vaá¹ƒÅ›Ävali as a genre takes as its focus telling a story about continuities, the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya, at least in the portion that survives, attends again and again to moments of discontinuity, centering its narrative first around the failure to secure an heir and then on the further failure to secure a worthy heir. When we encounter him, Ä€havamalla is victorious in battle and enjoys the intimate company of thousands of women beyond his wife. Without a dear son, although the house courtyard is radiant with sovereign fortune

[[P570]]

(laká¹£mÄ«), who is dancing accompanied by ringing of the bells, with female elephants with tinkling golden bells, and sensuous women moving playfully to the lovely music struck upon the tambourine and beaten upon the drumâ€”I see the courtyard as empty.92

In their hour of need, as eventually becomes transparent, the grieving couple lean upon the wrong sort of support. They go and ask the advice of BrÄhmaá¹‡as. SomeÅ›vara III, to be clear, is no strong critic of caste nor does he hold any particular antipathy for the existence of BrÄhmaá¹‡ical knowledge as such. As far as he is considered, these are things are fine, indeed sometimes necessary, but they belong in their own proper place. In the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya, in a radical departure from the vision of Bilhaá¹‡a, BrÄhmaá¹‡ical knowledge is consistently represented as overreaching in its ambitions and incomplete in its understanding, providing a miÅ›ra mixture of both sound and deeply stupid advice.

The master has sort of given himself over to ceaseless worry, but to what avail? What is there that cannot be accomplished by the performance of dÄna and tapas, correctly performed? What cannot be obtained from the best of BrÄhmaá¹‡as being satisfied? What is it that is hard to obtain when the Lord, the beloved of BhavÄnÄ«, is worshipped?

[[P571]]

The BrÄhmaá¹‡as begin obsequiously, but quickly their discourse moves on to a repeated conflation of the efficacy of the rituals with the pleasing of BrÄhmaá¹‡as. While a normal feature of the discourses related to vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma with their incessant invocations of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a as a god on earth, in SomeÅ›varaâ€™s writings, it is this attribution that is itself subject to a throughgoing problematization. The VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya, succinctly, sets out to demonstrate that, contrary to commonplace rhetoric, the pleasing of Lord Åšiva and the gratification of BrÄhmaá¹‡as are not the same thing. Indeed, our story unfolds as essentially a cautionary tale demonstrating that the very logic that animates the sponsoring of SmÄrta ritual95 is itself not truly consonant with the type of worship that wins Åšivaâ€™s favor. As we shall soon see, the CÄá¸·ukya kings operate in a world populated by discrete and site-specific religious modalities, each of which demands its own decorum. Indeed, the office of kingship

[[P572]] requires the ruler to seamlessly navigate between these distinctive realms several times in the very same day. What our text depicts is a domain in which such code-switching is both ubiquitous and usually carried out almost automatically, with very little self-reflexivity on the part of the ritual actor. Indeed, while there is a pragmatic awareness that different contexts demand different actions, there seems to be virtually no conscious understanding that this is because the different ritual domains instantiate distinctive values and ideals and demand from the ritual actor different sorts of ritual intentionality.

Momentarily convinced by his learned advisors, rising from his bed, Ä€havamalla goes about his morning routine under the guidance of his rÄjapurohita and his astrologer. After a brief detour to the house of the god where he conducts his morning nitya ritual duties in absolute privacy, returning to a more public space, the king takes a ritual bath. Coming to the court, seated on his throne, he receives the praise of his courtiers and gives out the daily philanthropy. As you can see, he directs much of the largesse to BrÄhmaá¹‡as. Ever so subtly foreshadowing the force of the narrative, our author seems to suggest that these pious folks have a number of rather laukika reasons, such as large families who will be needing dowries, for promoting that the best kind of ritual is one where BrÄhmaá¹‡as get huge payouts.

With Å›raddhÄ and great respect, having a good mind, he [the king] gave perpetual dÄna, including milk cows, gold, clothes, gems, elephants, horses, villages, and so forth, all of that stuff beyond measure, and that which was agreed upon by himself, to the best of the BrÄhmaá¹‡as, ones who came from other countries and those who were living in his own country, with lots of family members, who had established the sacrificial fires, who were engaged in performing weddings, upanayanas, and rituals, who had nice forms, who know the Vedas and the meaning of the Vedas and who have the conducts and the ÄcÄra of a well-examined family.96

The great king, having drunk that nectarean speech with the aÃ±jali-like cavities of his ears, thought of himself, at that very time, as someone who has produced a son.98

Serendipitously for the modern historian of religion, SomeÅ›vara III in his usual fashion eschews the euphemistic treatment of private worship ubiquitous in Sanskrit discourse, in its place offering a staggering glimpse of the ritual system that animated the greater CÄá¸·ukya Imperium.

[[P575]]

Much to our astonishment, lurking at the heart of CÄá¸·ukya power is nothing other than the veneration of the terrifying deity Bhairava unmistakably mediated through a heavily entextualized tradition rooted in Tantric practices. Quite apart from the normal trappings of lay Åšaiva pÅ«jÄ, which preserve the upasaá¹ƒhÄra program of worship of the old AtimÄrga PÄÅ›upatas, which privilege devotional music, Ä€havahamallaâ€™s obligatory ritual duties entail the use of mantra, mudrÄ and esoteric meditative practices. These procedures serve the purpose of transforming the king himself into a form of the god Svacchanda Bhairava.100 As his grandson

[[P576]] transparently tells us, these rituals are no mere calendrically inflected trappings loosely associated with the office. They represent the daily obligatory ritual procedure (nityavidhi) that the king is obligated to perform if he is to maintain his ritual status and authority. His capacity to engage in such procedures is almost certainly dependent on the CÄá¸·ukya king bearing an initiation into ÅšÄkta ritual system in which a male deity bearing the name of Svacchanda is worshipped. With the evidence available to us, however, there is as of yet no way of determining if the system in question is specifically the worship of Svacchandabhairava as prescribed by the Svacchanda Tantra. By the eleventh century, both the deity Svacchandabhairava and the textual resources provided by the Svacchanda Tantra, including the cosmologies and the meditative and mantric praxes it prescribes, have been claimed by religious communities across the entire spectrum of the traditions of the MantramÄrga. Just as the Svacchanda Tantra itself has drawn upon the proto Åšaiva SiddhÄnta foundational scripture, the NiÅ›vÄsaguhyasÅ«tra, value systems as disparate as those of the overtly doctrinally nondualist Trika TantrasadbhÄva, which advocates for the ritual worship of the yoginÄ«s, and the classically dualistic Åšaiva SiddhÄnta paddhati, the SiddhÄntasÄrÄvali ascribed to the famous ParamÄra king Bhojadeva of DhÄrÄ, which most assuredly does not, both extensively draw upon the Svacchandaâ€™s cosmological and mantra systems as a sort of Tantric source code for filling in the gaps in its own ritual system. As a central focus of worship, the deity himself is even more widely dispersed, surfacing for example in the KubjikÄ Tantras, especially in the form of ÅšikhÄsvacchandabhairava, in the alchemical texts in the guise of various iterations of Rasabhairava, and as the generic form of MahÄbhairava in chapter 20 of the

[[P577]] TantrasadbhÄva.101 Until further evidence emerges, it suffices to say that, having become the god, Ä€havamalla worships the god with his very own hands, making offerings to a private votary liá¹…ga made of moonstone (candrakantÄ) called a ÅšrÄ«kaá¹‡á¹­haliá¹…ga, a focus of worship absent from the surviving literature.102

In SomeÅ›varaâ€™s brief representation, this Tantric pÅ«jÄ is not marked as motivated by some deep personal commitment to the theology of a particular stream of esoteric revelation. Indeed, the pÅ«jÄ is conducted without any invocation of a ritual intention. What we call Tantra in this world, it seems, is simply part and parcel of royal office, something that has to be done for a CÄá¸·ukya king to remain a CÄá¸·ukya king. Furthermore, this esoteric modality is at once written into the daily routine and divorced from the transactional performances that fill the rest of the kingâ€™s schedule, for unlike all of the rest of the activities in which he is engaged, Ä€havahamalla performs this part of his duties on his own, free from both learned professional oversight on the one hand and sycophantic service on the other. As we shall see, the VikramÄá¹…kÄbhyudaya appears to be offering an argument by example that such an approach is the best way to worship Åšiva. Indeed, there is scarcely anything indicating that the rest of the kingâ€™s retinue has anything more than a vague understanding that the king observes some unspecified private ritual practice.

Ä€havamalla marks his completion of his private pÅ«jÄ by putting on clean clothes. Exiting the house of the god, he returns to work. He commissions the pavilions, made of

[[P578]] perishable materials, within which the sixteen mahÄdÄnas will be offered in the coming days. He gets his daily report concerning foreign affairs, alliances, and wars. The king receives the supplication of his long-standing vassals as well as of the princes whose kingdoms he has recently conquered, who shower him with all sorts of gifts, priceless cloths, gold, elephants, and horses, as they beg for his mercy and court his favor. In short, like the god he himself will soon supplicate, the king is showered with flattery and riches by those who require his favor to accomplish their own aims. When the day of the great potlatch at last arrives, Ä€havamalla assumes the role of master of ceremonies, directing lavish presents and praise at the Lord who is Åšiva, intent on purchasing an heir through his efforts.

[[P579]] In light of what will come after, it is perhaps worth noting at the outset that the key verbs in this passage, the paraphrastic perfect kÄrayÄmÄsa and the imperfect causative passive akÄryata impart a causative meaning, evoking a clear sense that the king is not himself doing the ritual, but merely having the ritual â€œdone for himâ€ by other people. After the completion of these very involved and expensive festivities, that very night, the CÄá¸·ukya king has a dream in which a vaguely menacing ascetic appears before him. With his mass of tawny locks and bark garment, his body smeared with ash, bearing the goad of Rudra and a flute, the yogin clearly stands in for Lord Åšiva himself. He imparts a devastating message. Expressing palpable disdain for the arrogance of thinking that one could buy godâ€™s favor as well as ridiculing the ridiculous amount of money wasted on the sixteen mahÄdÄnas, the king is told that his efforts have been utterly misguided and will result in terrible consequences.

O king, these austerities of yours are complete; in only a few days you will obtain a son. And yet, in just the same spirit that the worship of Lord Åšiva was performed by you, a son was produced of the very same sort. [As] your inner faculties were seized by a tempestuous state, [for] you had become haughty, drunk on philanthropic practices involving the excessive expenditure of vast amounts of money, you have produced a son dominated by an excess of tempest (rÄjas) and torpor (tÄmasa).104

In place of the notion that all you have to do is please the BrÄhmaá¹‡as to get your way, Åšivaâ€™s vessel here invokes an older and more fundamental logic, which by now should be familiar to us as it permeates the discursive worlds we have been exploring at a myriad of registers: the ethical and intentional circumstances in which a thing or person comes into being

[[P580]] determines its intrinsic ontological nature. Far from representing mere ritual formula, the intentions and mental attitudes cultivated by humans and other types of beings create the character of the world they inhabit. The king and his wife, with their considerable academic understanding of the conditionable nature of the human mind and human character, at once realize the wisdom conveyed in this vision, and repent, lamenting their own foolishness. Here the king tells his soon to be pregnant wife the very bad news.

O dear one, the ripening of bad deeds previously committed is difficult to overcome. Even though my mental habits had been naturally predominated by sattva since birth, since this bhÄva came into being precisely on the force of an excess of rajas on my part, as I was engaged in the worship of my chosen deity for the sake of obtaining offspring, today, in fact, in the course of sleep, I was informed by Åšiva that â€œyour son will also be rÄjasic, for your mental operations had a rÄjasic state.â€ And thus, my heart is overcome, possessed by boundless sorrow.

Better than this obtaining of a bad son would be having no son at all.

Then again, a son with bad conduct is precisely the cause of affliction for the father, here in this world and the world beyond. . . . For, just like a sharp axe, he severs the branches that are the fame of the family that had flourished far and wide. For, the excess of rajas, like a hurricane, assails the eyes of all the people. For, the conduct of inertia that is not dispersed stains the foot like mud. For, one who is maddened by intoxication goes about just like an elephant without a goad. Throughout the queenâ€™s pregnancy, as is often the case in kÄvya, the malignant nature of her future child is readily apparent. In keeping with the circumstances of his production,

With the passing of time, just like kali, he grew stronger. For no reason, although they were good folks, the prince intensely assailed his play companions. Rather than acquiescing to this horrible little monster someday coming into power, Ä€havamalla and his wife set out to secure a second superior son. Practicing that kind of tapas, exceedingly extreme; that which causes adversity to the senses, which drives away the sense objects;

Excessive in your expectation, because you are not satisfied despite the obtaining of a son who will conquer the world, you have manifested a tÄmasic essence. Thus, another son will come into being who is made up of tamas, just like poison. Therefore, quickly return now to your own city.115

Like a disobedient child sent to his room, Ä€havamalla returns to his capital city just in time to start a family of his own. Sadly, this is just about where the extant text ends, offering us just a taste of the respective childhoods of the various princes. About VikramÄditya VI we learn very little of substance.116 In contradistinction to his dastardly brothers, from birth onward he was both polite and righteous. When his horoscope is done, to the astrologerâ€™s astonishment, his splendor and destiny is seen to both resemble and rival his great ancestor Tailapa, uprooter of the RÄá¹£á¹­rakÅ«á¹­as, which in fact is hardly surprising, given that both men are understood to be incarnations of NÄrÄyaá¹‡a himself. Perhaps the only feature worth noting in the presentation of the early ritual life of VikramÄditya VI as he receives the Vaidika saá¹ƒskÄras, each of which are described in loving poetic detail, and prepares for studentship is that, alongside the detailed delineations of the princeâ€™s Vaidika vidhÄna, if just in passing, our text alludes to the private performance of a separate set of ritual procedures, which VikramÄditya VI dedicates to his kuladevatÄ.

[[P588]] In making sense of the hidden ritual life of the great CÄá¸·ukya sovereign who ruled the realm in relative peace for fifty years, overseeing a renaissance in the visual culture of the medieval Deccan, whose influence was felt into the thirteenth century, as we might by now anticipate, Bilhaá¹‡a, his official biographer is of no help whatsoever. His VikramÄditya VI is a thoroughly idealized instantiation of generalized virtues, whose theological leanings are entirely opaque. Thankfully, once again, SomeÅ›vara III comes to our rescue, offering us a tantalizing detail about his fatherâ€™s â€œhobbies.â€ This time our source is not his sonâ€™s mahÄkÄvya, but rather his significantly more famous and yet equally under examined compendium of knowledge, the MÄnasollÄsa.117

The fifth book of the MÄnasollÄsa offers a catalog of what are typically taken to represent â€œcourtly enjoyments.â€ The Sanskrit word used, krÄ«á¸Ä, has a substantively different semantic field, and in content the chapter encompasses activities as varied as planting trees, to hosting parties in which men and women consume liquor until the point of passing out, but for the most part, the subject at hand seems to correspond to â€œsecularâ€ forms of recreation.

The eighteenth krÄ«á¸Ä, in contrast, outlines a very different sort of activity: vÄ«rakrÄ«á¸Ä, the midnight revelries of the initiated ÅšÄkta adept. It is here, tucked away at the end of his vast compendium, that SomeÅ›vara III offers some candid biographical reflection on the true nature of his father.

[[P589]]

Now is stated the vÄ«ra revelry, which delights the minds of the steadfast. The revelry is made manifest by prior men who sought to attain siddhis. Therefore, that is to be done by virile men, and not by other kings. It is enacted by the great impetuous one (mahÄsahÄsika), who bears the form of VikrÄmaditya. . . who is the best among the vÄ«ras. It was shown by the lord of the virile ones (vÄ«ranÄtha), the king of the ÅšÄlivÄhana, son of KÄntÄ«Å›vara, who was served by KÄlidÄsa. And other various vÄ«ras played (khelitÄ) upon the surface of this earth. By that play, they all obtained great siddhi.118

SomeÅ›vara IIIâ€™s verse is subtle in its implications, but it seems to speak of at least two distinctive agents.119 On the one hand, there is the VÄ«ranÄtha of the ÅšÄlivÄhana age who has KÄlidÄsa in his service, who â€œshowedâ€ the revelry. This is clearly the quasi-mythical VikramÄditya of Ujjain, who features as the protagonist of two collections of kathÄ literature, namely, the Vampire Tales and the Stories of the Thrones. Here, he has been slightly reimagined from old style power-seeking sÄdhaka into an adept of the yoginÄ«kula. On the other, there is the mahÄsahÄsika, best among the vÄ«ras, who merely enacted the rite. This one, who bears the form of VikramÄditya is none other than the CÄá¸·ukya emperor, VikramÄditya VI, who, his own son has just told us, was an initiated ÅšÄkta adept committed enough to his practice have attained the exalted ritual authority necessary, as we shall see, to sport with the yoginÄ«s themselves. To get a sense of what a commitment would have entailed, it is worth our time to examine in some

[[P590]] detail the revelry of the ÅšÄkta adept, whose â€œplayâ€ is an act of mimesis of the terrifying revelries of the great god Bhairava himself.

The rite seems to be subdivided into two conceptually distinct parts. The first involves the attaining of various powers through summoning and subjugating various classes of predatory quasi demonic beings. The second involves congress with the yoginÄ«s, culminating in making offering of flesh before the throne of the supreme goddess herself.

The sword, arrow, club, vessel, stick, clenched fist, canvas. Ointment, magical sandals, gem, vetÄla, finger ring, and rasa. The ointment that allows one to see treasure (nidhi), coins, gambling die, and the power of speech. These are the eighteen siddhis; I will tell the characteristics of each of them in sequence.120

Just as it promises, the text then proceeds to outline the meaning of this rather eccentric list of magical powers. For our purposes, we need only treat a few as representatives. The siddhi of the arrow (bÄá¹‡a) makes it so that the arrows one fires, after hitting their target, are returned to the hand of the archer. The siddhi of the vessel (pÄtrasiddhi) makes it so whatever you wish for appears in you begging bowl.121 The siddhi of the fist (muá¹£á¹­hisiddhi), â€œwhich destroys the poverty of the world,â€ is essentially the touch of Midas. Paá¹­asiddhi makes you invisible; khaá¹­i siddhi makes whatever you write down come true; pÄdukÄ makes it so you can travel with your new pair of sandals to any country in an instant; vetÄla siddhi gives you mastery over a vetÄla, and rasasiddhi makes it so that the body overcomes old age and death, just as iron is converted into gold.122 In short, these are useful abilities with a range of military

[[P591]] and practical applications and it is easy to see why even an already powerful ruler would desire to obtain such capacities. Any temptation most of us might have to write away for the relevant correspondence course, however, is unlikely to survive the application process that the text continues on to outline.

Therefore, it is to be enacted by a vÄ«ra who has the conduct of a real tiger, the steadfast one who is pure and true, who knows weapons and the mantras.

When half of the watch of the night is gone, in a city devoid of people walking about, with the assistance of sword and armor, he may go about unseen by anyone. He recollects the lord Narahari, the slayer of obstacles without remainder. Having meditated on Åšiva, whose nature is pacification, the King, having meditated on the Lord of the bhÅ«tas, having borne Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ, the destroyer of all enemies, in his heart, he praises VinÄyaka, the lord who destroys all obstacles. The king, having gone out from the house, attentively, should go quietly to a run-down temple. . . or a crossroads. He goes to a wide banyan tree or pipal tree that is very spread out, or likewise to the bank of lotus pond, [or] also to ancient groves, [or], his inner heart emboldened, to the middle of a river. [He will then see the]:

PiÅ›Äcas, guhyakas, yaká¹£as, vetÄlas, great gaá¹‡as, rÄká¹£asas, great flesh eaters, pretas, and the fathers who live in the forest, the ones with flaming mouths, with great bodies. . . . The ones who are bald, flaming, crooked, long, short, ones with a big roar, ones made of skin and bones, poor, ones with great mouths, very terrifying long teeth, fierce faced, big tongues, big bellies. Some with three feet, some have one foot, some bear long heads. Some have eyes that are hollow holes, Others are ones whose eyes have been removed, or are licking very intensely.123

[[P592]] The reason why our text is tediously emphatic that only a professional ÅšÄkta adept with a focused mind who is certain in his courage should undertake such efforts is that, as any number of Tantras or, for that matter, experience with wild predators, make evident, the slightest hesitation and these terrible things, the appearance of whom is enough to drive lesser folks crazy, were believed to tear you bits. In his preparatory practices, the king has ritually identified himself in sequence with Narasiá¹ƒha, with Åšiva, quite specifically in the form of the master of spirits, with goddess Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ and finally with the lord of the gaá¹‡as. In other words, he has done his best to establish himself as ritually non-different from the various sorts of higher order authorities that these types of beings view as their masters. As the text makes plain, this initial show of dominance is the only way the adept can begin the process of binding these things to his will.

Having seen these various sorts of deformed things, by means of giving food, the virile one endowed with delight124 should incite the desire of these meat eaters afflicted by hunger. The king should threaten those ones with terrible forms, and should assuage those who are afflicted. Some vetÄlas, when assailed by [his] valor, will go to servitude. Some other beings will become his servants when appeased by flesh offerings. Others, likewise, when pacified, will become his servants out of gratitude.

Such is the virility (Å›aurya) and heroism (sÄhasa) of the king and his gallant power.125

This is a difficult passage and much about it remains obscure. The gist of the matter, however, seems to be that the demonic denizens of the night, the literal niÅ›acÄras, can be won

[[P593]] to oneâ€™s service through a display of courage and feeding them meat. Depending on the species at hand, as our text helpfully point out, however, the proper tactics one ought to deploy differ, especially in regard to how much of a display of violent force is necessary before getting oneâ€™s way. VetÄlas, it seems, are particularly stubborn in this regard and demand a good initial whacking if you wish to win their assistance. Once their spirits are broken, different types of spirits make more or less agreeable slaves. Some genuinely attend to your needs and serve your interests. Others merely submit to carrying out specific commands. In any case, once you have filled the ranks of your otherworldly staff for cremation ground practices, it becomes possible to move on to the highest kind of practice, what the ÅšÄkta Tantras call the melana:

[He then sees] them, abiding on the outskirts of the groves of the ancestors, assembled for the sake of forest revelry. . . .

Many yoginÄ«s have arisen: those born not born from a womb and those born from the field, yoginÄ«s arising spontaneously, ones arisen from samaya, the secret ones, and the manifest ones.

The first pÄ«á¹­ha is JÄlandhara, the second is KÄmarÅ«pa, the third one is Uá¸á¸iyÄá¹‡a, the fourth one is UjjayanÄ«. The main field is Kolhapur. There is KarahÄá¹­a, VirÄá¹­a, PÅ«rá¹‡agiri, ÅšrÄ«giri, and Mukoá¹­aka,126 ÅšambhuÅ›ikhara, the Vindhyas and CitrakÅ«á¹­a, and NihÄsaka, EkÄmraka, and Tibet, and PrÄyaga, the Malaya [mountains], and YelÄrapura. The ká¹£etras are of these various sorts. And yoginÄ«s have arisen there that were not born from a womb or were born from a pÄ«á¹­ha.

The yoginÄ«s born in a field are known as those â€œborn from a field.â€ The yoginÄ«s endowed with various sorts of powers by practices in other births, who take various forms in whichever other lifetimes, are known of as sahajÄ. The yoginÄ«s [have come forth from the samaya] because of samaya conduct and samaya dÄ«ká¹£Ä.

There are two types that have come forth from the samaya: born from the pÄ«á¹­ha and born from the field. They are twofold, on the basis of the division between secret and manifest. Wives, slaves, prostitutes, widows, and ascetics: they are not marked by their own actions, these ones are secret, and known to be fivefold.

Those yoginÄ«s are known to be manifest that are yogamÄá¹­rs, á¸Äkinis, pÅ«tanÄs, Å›ÄkinÄ«s, and rÅ«pikÄs. Having made their power manifest, they go about everywhere. RevatÄ«s,

[[P594]]

Å›uá¹£karevatÄ«s, the ones adorned with fierce horse headsâ€”they are installed all around at the end of the DevÄ«â€™s lion throne.

The ones who are concealed yoginÄ«s reveal their own form. They have a non- anthropomorphic form, adorned by gold and beauty. They are abounding in similarity to non-anthropomorphic beings. Sprinkled with pure water, they are seated on a divine throne, resplendent with a yak tail fan and umbrella.127

From our perspective, SomeÅ›varaâ€™s byzantine speciation of the types of yoginÄ«s can be distilled down to the following observation.128 The category of yoginÄ«s is comprised out of

[[P595]] what to us seem like three distinct types of entities. First, there are things that are obviously yoginÄ«s, namely, entities belonging to classes of supernatural beings of a rather menacing nature who come into existence under a range of circumstances. Second, there are human women, who acquire this status either from birth or through the process of initiation. The social role of these â€œhidden yoginÄ«sâ€ could be that of a wife, a slave, a prostitute, a widow, or an ascetic. Finally, and most surprisingly, there are inanimate objects, such as the pÄtra vessels into which goddesses are frequently found invoked worshipped in lieu of images even in the modern Deccan. In a somewhat impressionistic way, the sum toto of this canon of entities is associated with a composite sacred landscape in which the standard set of pÄ«á¹­has is emended so as to incorporate distinctively Deccani sites.129 In place of the more intricate cosmic hierarchies and (female authority) pecking orders one often encounters in the Tantras, here, the relative priority and authority of the different types of yoginÄ«s, be they monster, women, or water pot, seems immaterial, for all of them are to be addressed collectivity in the very same way:

The king, being not agitated, having seen that marvelous divine form, seated on the lion throne, should propitiate the goddesses with blood and flesh. Having bowed with bhakti, without fear, he should worship with mantras. Then, the goddesses, being pleased by his heroism, bestow siddhis. That lotus-eyed [king] takes up all of the siddhis that were highly desired. Thus, having obtained the siddhis, the king should go to his own temple.

In the morning, showing the people his attainment of siddhi, he is delighted.130 Thus is described the terrifying vÄ«ra revelry, which causes happiness to arise.131

Given what we have seen thus far, that the king should conclude a successful evening of phantasmagorical revelry by retiring to the ritual center attached to his own home, presumably to ritually and emotionally decompress from his harrowing affair, is perhaps unsurprising. After all, liturgically speaking, the liminal terrain through which he just traversed and the types of imagined spaces that would have been invoked and projected into the ritual space are not terribly different. SomeÅ›varaâ€™s concluding remark, in contrast, should give us pause, for with it he suggests that there was some measure for the public acknowledgement and even adulation of what we have always been inclined to conceive of as among the most clandestine and highly transgressive of esoteric rituals. It is to this strange dynamic of hiding a secret in plain sight, of mustering a tacit acknowledgement on the part of a wider public unconcerned with delving into the details, to which we will next turn our attention.
